abbreviated form is provided.

- Domain subtraction/antirestriction ::   $X ‚©§ R \;=\; \{a : A ; b : B \;‚ùô\; a
  „Äî R„Äï b ‚àß a ‚àâ X ‚Ä¢ ‚ü®a, b‚ü© \}$.

  This includes only those edges that /do not start in $X$./

- Range subtraction/antirestriction ::
    $R ‚©• Y \;=\; \{a : A ; b : B \;‚ùô\; a „Äî R„Äï b ‚àß b ‚àâ Y ‚Ä¢ ‚ü®a, b‚ü© \}$.

  This includes only those edges that /do not end in $Y$./

#+begin_box The types
- $\_{}‚©§\_{} : ‚Ñô A ‚Üí (A ‚Üî B) ‚Üí (A ‚Üî B)$
- $\_{}‚©•\_{} : (A ‚Üî B) ‚Üí ‚Ñô B ‚Üí (A ‚Üî B)$
#+end_box

*** Relational Image and Cardinality
    :PROPERTIES:
    :CUSTOM_ID: Relational-Image
    :END:

It may be that we are interested in the effect of a relation upon a particular
set of elements. The /relational image/ of a set $X : ‚Ñô A$ under $R : A ‚Üî B$ is
the set $R‚¶áX‚¶à$ of all elements in $B$ to which some element of $X$ is related;
i.e., it is the range of $R$ domain restricted to $X$.

|   | $R‚¶áX‚¶à$                                         |
| /=/ | $\Ran (X ‚óÅ R)$                                 |
| /=/ | $\{a :A; b : B \;‚ùô\; a „ÄîR„Äï b  ‚àß a ‚àà X ‚Ä¢ b\}$ |

As a graph, $R‚¶áX‚¶à$ is the end-points of all edges that start in the set of nodes
$X$.  That is,
- $R‚¶áX‚¶à$ is the ‚Äòsuccessors‚Äô of $X$  ---the nodes that start-in/go-out-of X.
- $RÀò‚¶áX‚¶à$ is the ‚Äòpredecessors‚Äô of $X$ ---the nodes that end-up-in $X$

#+begin_box The type
$\_{}‚¶á\_{}‚¶à : (A ‚Üî B) ‚Üí ‚Ñô A ‚Üí ‚Ñô B$
#+end_box

When $R$ is a relation between finite sets, we define $‚à• R ‚à• \;=\; (‚Üë y ‚Ä¢ ‚ôØ\{x
\;‚ùô\; x „ÄîR„Äï y\})$ to be the maximum number of elements in relation to an
element. As a matrix, $‚à•R‚à•$ is the maximum number of 1s in a column.
Let $|S|$ be the number of elements in a set $S$, then:

1. $|R ‚¶áX‚¶à | ‚â§ ‚à•R‚à• ¬∑ |X|$
2. $‚à•RÀò‚à• ‚â§ 1$ and $|RÀò‚¶áX‚¶à| ‚â§ |X|$, if $R$ is univalent.

   Why? If $R$ is univalent, then every element has at most one /R/-predecessor;
   so if we look at the predecessors of a set $X$, there are only be at most
   $|X|$-many of them.

3. $‚à•RÀò‚à• = 1$ and $|RÀò‚¶áX‚¶à| ‚â§ |X|$, if $R$ is a mapping.
4. $‚à•RÀò‚à• = 1$ and $|RÀò‚¶áX‚¶à| = |X|$, if $R$ is an injective mapping.

*** Theorems
    :PROPERTIES:
    :CUSTOM_ID: Theorems
    :END:
#+begin_box
1. $\Dom R ‚óÅ R = R$
2. $X ‚óÅ R = R ‚à© (X √ó ùëº)$ ---recall that ‚Äò√ó‚Äô distributes over both ‚Äò‚à™‚Äô and ‚Äò‚à©‚Äô
3. $X ‚©§ R = R ‚à© (\sim X √ó ùëº)$
4. $R ‚ñ∑ Y = R ‚à© (ùëº √ó Y)$
5. $R ‚©• Y = R ‚à© (ùëº √ó \sim Y)$
6. $(X ‚à© Y) ‚óÅ R = (X ‚óÅ R) ‚à© (Y ‚óÅ R)$
7. $X ‚óÅ (R ‚à™ S) = (X ‚óÅ R) ‚à™ (Y ‚óÅ S)$
8. $R ‚ñ∑ Y = (Y ‚óÅ R Àò) Àò$
9. $X ‚óÅ R = (R Àò ‚ñ∑ X) Àò$
10. $R ‚ñ∑ (X ‚à© Y) = (R ‚ñ∑ X) ‚à© (R ‚ñ∑ Y)$
11. $(R ‚à™ S) ‚ñ∑ Y = (R ‚ñ∑ Y) ‚à™ (S ‚ñ∑ Y)$
12. $\Ran (R ‚ñ∑ Y) = \Ran R ‚à© Y$
13. ‚òÖ $X ‚óÅ R = \Id_X ‚®æ R$
14. ‚òÖ $R ‚ñ∑ Y = R ‚®æ \Id_Y$
15. ‚ÄúSwitcheroo‚Äù: $(R ‚ñ∑ Y) ‚®æ S = R ‚®æ (Y ‚óÅ S)$
    - By extensionality, this requires some work, but by (‚òÖ)
      this is trivial!
16. ‚ÄúMutual associativity of ‚®æ with ‚ñ∑‚Äù: $(R ‚®æ S) ‚ñ∑ Z = R ‚®æ (S ‚ñ∑ Z)$
#+end_box
** COMMENT Relation Override
   :PROPERTIES:
   :CUSTOM_ID: COMMENT-Relation-Override
   :END:

$R ‚äï S = (\Dom S ‚©§ R) ‚à™ S$

#+begin_src math
    x ‚¶ó R ‚äï S ‚¶ò y  ‚â°  if x ‚àà Dom S then x ‚¶ó S ‚¶ò y else x ‚¶ó R ‚¶ò y fi
#+end_src

** Converse ---‚ÄúTranspose‚Äù
   :PROPERTIES:
   :CUSTOM_ID: Converse
   :END:

Viewed as a simple bipartite graph, a relation ‚Äúnaturally comes with an
interesting operation‚Äù: A relation is a bunch of arrows from dots on the left to
dots on the right, what if /we change perspective/ and see the arrows ‚Äúgoing the
other way around‚Äù?

The *converse* of a relation /R : A ‚Üî B/ is the relation $RÀò : B ‚Üî A$ consisting of
the converse of the relationships of /R/; i.e., $b „Äî RÀò„Äï a \;‚â°\; a „ÄîR„Äï b$.

For example, if /Jim „Äî parentOf „Äï Kalthum/ then /Kalthum „Äî parentOfÀò „Äï Jim/ and
we may as well define /childOf = parentOfÀò/. That is,
‚ÄúJim is the parent of Kalthum‚Äù ‚â° ‚ÄúKalthum is a child of Jim‚Äù.
*Converse* means to change the order of the related items.

#+begin_box Examples
Colloquially, converse is often expressed using separate wordings:
| Bob ‚Äúis taller than‚Äù Chris    | ‚â° | Chris ‚Äúis shorter than‚Äù Bob     |
| Jasim ‚Äúis the boss of‚Äù Jason  | ‚â° | Jason ‚Äúis an employee of‚Äù Jasim |
| Mariam ‚Äúbuys from‚Äù Mary       | ‚â° | Mary ‚Äúsells to‚Äù Mariam          |
| Musa ‚Äúis the parent of‚Äù Yusuf | ‚â° | Yusuf ‚Äúis the child of‚Äù Musa    |
| Wolfram ‚Äúworks with‚Äù Jacques  | ‚â° | Jacques ‚Äúworks with‚Äù Wolfram    |

Notice that the final example, intuitively, has a relation that is its own
converse ---i.e., /R Àò = R/--- we will return to these later as ‚Äòsymmetric
relationships‚Äô.
#+end_box

Intuitively,
- $R Àò Àò = R$
- $\Dom (R Àò) = \Ran R$
- $\Ran (R Àò) = \Dom R$.
- (CCC) Converse commutes with complementation: $(\sim R)Àò \;=\; \sim (R Àò)$
- $R ‚äÜ S \;‚â°\; R Àò ‚äÜ SÀò$
- Converse commutes with joins and meets: $(R ‚à© S)Àò \;=\; R Àò ‚à© SÀò$
  and $(R ‚à™ S)Àò \;=\; R Àò ‚à™ SÀò$
- Identity, empty, and universal relations stay the same when transposed:
  $\Id Àò = \Id, \; ‚ä•Àò = ‚ä•,\; ‚ä§ Àò = ‚ä§$.

#+begin_box The type
$\_{}Àò : (A ‚Üî B) ‚Üí (B ‚Üî A)$

Relations are directed and converse /changes/ the direction.
#+end_box

** Constructing new relations from existing relations
   :PROPERTIES:
   :CUSTOM_ID: Constructing-new-relations-from-existing-relations
   :END:

Since relations are sets of pairs, the usual set theory operations
---intersection, union, complement, inclusion--- can be used to construct new
relations from existing ones.

In particular, if we ‚Äúplace side-by-side two relations, sharing a common type‚Äù
$R : A ‚Üî B$ and $S : B ‚Üî C$, then, as graphs, we need to only draw $B$ /once/ and
let the graphs of $R$ and $S$ /share/ it. One then immediately realises that an
element of $A$ may be /R/-related to an element of $B$ which is itself /S/-related
to an element of $C$; i.e., we have a new relation between $A$ and $C$.  This is
the *composition of R and S*, it is denoted $R ‚®æ S$ ---and may be read /‚ÄúR and then
S‚Äù/.

The *composition* is defined by $a „ÄîR ‚®æ S„Äïc \;‚â°\; (‚àÉ b : B ‚Ä¢ a „ÄîR„Äïb „ÄîS„Äï c)$.

#+begin_box Family Examples
1. ‚Äúis father of‚Äù ‚®æ ‚Äúis parent of‚Äù = ‚Äúis grandfather of‚Äù
2. ‚Äúis brother of‚Äù ‚®æ ‚Äúis parent of‚Äù = ‚Äúis uncle of‚Äù
3. ‚Äúis parent of‚Äù Àò = ‚Äúis child of‚Äù
4. ‚Äúis child of‚Äù ‚à© ‚Äúis a boy‚Äù = ‚Äúis son of‚Äù
   + ‚Äúis a boy‚Äù is the relation defined by /x „Äîis a boy„Äï y ¬†‚â°¬† x is a boy/, no
     constraint on /y/ ---we will see these kinds of relation later as ‚Äòvectors‚Äô.
5. ‚Äúis son of‚Äù ‚®æ (‚Äúis brother of‚Äù ‚à™ ‚Äúis sister of‚Äù) = ‚Äúis nephew of‚Äù
#+end_box

Intuitively,
- For $R : A ‚Üî B$, the elements of $A$ affected by $R$ are captured by the
  relation $R ‚®æ ‚ä§$, whereas $RÀò ‚®æ ‚ä§$ refers to the elements of $B$ affected by
  $R$.
  + Later we will see relations of the form $Q ‚®æ ‚ä§$ as ‚Äúvectors‚Äù; i.e.,
    *sets disguised as relations*.
- Composition is an associative operation: $Q ‚®æ (R ‚®æ S) = (Q ‚®æ R) ‚®æ S$.
- It has the identity relation (loops only) as unit element and
  the empty relation (no edges at all) as the zero element:
  $\Id ‚®æ R = R ‚®æ \Id = R$ and $‚ä• ‚®æ R = R ‚®æ ‚ä• = ‚ä•$.
- Composition preserves containment: $R ‚äÜ R‚Ä≤ ‚àß S ‚äÜ S‚Ä≤ ‚áí R ‚®æ S ‚äÜ R‚Ä≤ ‚®æ S‚Ä≤$
- Composition distributes over unions, but only *sub*-distributes over
  intersections: $Q ‚®æ (R ‚à© S) \quad‚äÜ\quad Q ‚®æ R ‚à© Q ‚®æ S$.

  We have sub-distributivity since otherwise the following little calculation
  would give $‚ä• = ‚ä§$: $‚ä• = ‚ä§ ‚®æ ‚ä• = ‚ä§ ‚®æ (\Id ‚à© \sim\Id) ‚äÜ ‚ä§‚®æ\Id ‚à© ‚ä§‚®æ\sim\Id = ‚ä§ ‚à© ‚ä§ = ‚ä§$.

  #+begin_details Why subdistributivity?

  In a group of people that have at most one child ---i.e., /Q/ is ‚Äòunivalent‚Äô---
  the following is true
  |   | People with a blond son                          |
  | /=/ | People with a son ¬†¬†‚à©¬†¬†¬†People with a blond child |

  If anyone has more than one child, then it is possible that this is /not/ true:
  A person may have a dark-haired son as well as a blond daughter.

  In general, we have ‚Äú‚äÜ‚Äù instead of ‚Äú=‚Äù.
  #+end_details

- *Tarski rule:* Non-empty relations actually relate things; i.e.,
  $R ‚â† ‚ä• \;‚â°\; ‚ä§ ‚®æ R ‚®æ ‚ä§ = ‚ä§$ ---equivalently,
  every relation relates any source element to some target element
  /or/ does not relate a target element to any source:
  $R ‚®æ ‚ä§ = ‚ä§ ‚à® ‚ä§ ‚®æ \sim R = ‚ä§$
  (i.e., every relation is total or its negation is surjective).

  ( There are implementations of these operations that do /not/ satisfy
  this rule since the elements /cannot/ be conceived as sets of pairs.
  See [[http://relmics.mcmaster.ca/~kahl/Publications/TR/2000-02/][here]] for more. )

  Consequently, the universal relation is annihilated by the empty relation
  only: $R ‚®æ ‚ä§ = ‚ä• \quad‚â°\quad R = ‚ä•$.

  ( Likewise, $R ‚â† ‚ä• \;‚â°\; R Àò ‚®æ R ‚â† ‚ä•$. )
  # The relations R, R Àò ‚®æ R, and R Àò ‚®æ R vanish simultaneously.

  Moreover, since $‚ä§ ‚®æ ‚ä§ ‚®æ ‚ä§ = ‚ä§$ (in a homogenous setting) Tarski's rule shows that $‚ä§ ‚â† ‚ä•$.
  ( Tarski's rule assumes a non-empty universe. Indeed, in the extreme case that the universe is empty, then, and only then,
  do we have the degenerate case with $‚ä§ = ‚ä•$. )

When $R$ is homogeneous, then we define $R¬π = R$ and $R‚Åø‚Å∫¬π = R ‚®æ R‚Åø$.
Then, $x „Äî R‚Åø „Äï y$ precisely when the simple graph $R$ has a path of length /n/
from /x/ to /y/; i.e., there is a sequence of /n/-many nodes /p/ such that
/p‚ÇÄ = x/ and /‚àÄ i : 0..n-1 ‚Ä¢ p·µ¢ „ÄîR„Äï p·µ¢‚Çä‚ÇÅ/ and /p‚Çô‚Çã‚ÇÅ = y/.
The *type of paths of length n in simple graph R is denotes R‚Åø*; this is an
inductive data type.
- For more on this idea, see [[https://alhassy.github.io/PathCat.html][Graphs are to categories as lists are to monoids]].

#+begin_box The type
$\_{}‚®æ\_{} : (A ‚Üî B) ‚Üí (B ‚Üî C) ‚Üí (A ‚Üî C)$

The composition of relations is a generalisation of the composition of mappings.
In contrast to mappings, however, relations associates /sets/ of values with
elements. These sets may be empty, or contain one, or more, elements.

Think /f(x)/ versues $R‚¶á\{x\}‚¶à$.
- $(f ‚àò g)(x) \;=\; f (g(x))$
- $(R ‚®æ S)‚¶á\{x\}‚¶à \;=\; (‚à™ y \,‚ùô\, x „ÄîR„Äï y ‚Ä¢ S‚¶á\{y\}‚¶à)$
  - When $R$ is a mapping, the range becomes an equality and the one-point rule
    applies thereby eliminating the union and we get closer to the mapping
    composition.  If $S$ is also a mapping, then the relational image is a
    singleton and we have mapping composition again.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

The semicolon ‚Äò‚®æ‚Äô has a similar meaning as that of composing, sequencing,
programs in a programming language.
#+end_box

#+begin_box Matrix Multiplication = Relation Composition
Matrix product and relation product *are the same* when we write $R_{xy}, ¬∑, +$
in-place of $x „ÄîR„Äïy, ‚àß, ‚à®$, and use classical notation: \[ (R ‚®æ S)_{xz}
\;=\; \substack{{\Huge\exists} \\ \\ {}_{y}} \, R_{xy} ‚àß S_{yz} \] \[ (A ¬∑
B)_{ik} \;=\; \sum_j \, A_{ij} ¬∑ B_{jk} \]

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------


Many matrix laws are true for relations; e.g.,
the transpose matrix rule $(A ¬∑ B)^T = B^T ¬∑ A^T$
is the same as the relation converse rule $(R ‚®æ S)Àò = S Àò ‚®æ R Àò$.
#+end_box

** Properties of /Homogeneous/ Relations
   :PROPERTIES:
   :CUSTOM_ID: Properties-of-Homogeneous-Relations
   :END:

- Reflexive :: /Elements are related to themselves/

  /Everything is related to itself/.

  # A relation is reflexive if it includes the identity.
  |   | $R$ is reflexive |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ x ‚Ä¢ x „ÄîR„Äï x/  |
  | /=/ | $\Id ‚äÜ R$        |

- Transitive :: /Things related to things that are related, are themselves related./

    That is, whenever /x/ is relate to /y/ and /y/ is related to /z/, then also /x/ will
    be related to /z/.

    |   | $R$ is transitive                               |
    | /=/ | /‚àÄ x, y, z ‚Ä¢  x „Äî R „Äï y „ÄîR „Äï z  ‚áí  x „ÄîR„Äï z/ |
    | /=/ | $R ‚®æ R ‚äÜ R$                                     |

    A transitive relation is irreflexive precisely when it is asymmetric.

- Symmetric :: /The relationship is mutual; if one thing is related to the other,
  then the other is also related to the first./

  ( A relation is symmetric if it includes its converse: If /x/ is related to /y/, then /y/ is also related to /x/. )

  |   | $R$ is symmetric                |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢ x „ÄîR„Äï y ‚áí y „Äî R„Äï x/ |
  | /=/ | $R Àò ‚äÜ R$                       |
  | /=/ | $R ‚à© RÀò ‚äÜ R$                    |
  | /=/ | $R Àò = R$                       |

  As graphs, symmetric relations contains either exactly two arrows ---in
  opposite directions--- between any two elements or none at all.  As such, for
  clarity, one prefers ‚Äúsqueezing any two arrows in opposite directions‚Äù into
  one ‚Äòundirected‚Äô line and so obtains *undirected graphs*.
  - Undirected edges represent pairs of arrows pointing in opposite directions.

    Coreflexives are symmetric: $R ‚äÜ \Id ‚áí R Àò = R$.

- Antisymmetric :: /Different elements cannot be mutually related./

  Such relations allow us to prove equality between two elements;
  we have only to show that the relationship holds in both directions.
  * E.g, one often shows two sets are equal by using the antisymmetry of ‚Äò‚äÜ‚Äô.

  |   | $R$ is antisymmetric                             |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢ x „ÄîR„Äï y  ‚àß  y „Äî R„Äï x ‚áí x = y/        |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢  x ‚â† y  ‚áí  ¬¨ (x „ÄîR„Äï y  ‚àß  y „Äî R„Äï x)/ |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢  x ‚â† y  ‚áí  x „ÄîRÃ∏„Äï y  ‚à®  y „Äî RÃ∏„Äï x/     |
  | /=/ | $R ‚à© R Àò ‚äÜ \Id$                                  |
  | /=/ | $R Àò ‚äÜ \sim R ‚à™ \Id$                             |
  | /=/ | /R ‚ï≥ R = Id/  ---‚Äò‚ï≥‚Äô is introduced below after ‚Äò‚ï≤‚Äô |

  ( As a simple graph, an antisymmetric relation has /at most/ one arrow between
  any two different nodes. )

- Asymmetric :: /The relationship is mutually exclusive./

  ( That is, $x „Äî R„Äï y$ and $y „ÄîR„Äï x$ cannot both be true.

  Reflexive relations ---when /x = y/--- are thus not asymmetric. )

  |   | $R$ is asymmetric                   |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢ x „ÄîR„Äï y  ‚áí  ¬¨ y „Äî R„Äï x/ |
  | /=/ | $R ‚à© R Àò ‚äÜ ‚ä•$                       |
  | /=/ | $R Àò ‚äÜ \sim R$                      |
  # ? | /=/ | $R = \sim R Àò$                      |

  Asymmetrics are irreflexive ---just pick /x = y/ in the above ‚àÄ-formulation ;-)

Notice that a relation need not be any of Symmetric, Antisymmetric, nor
Asymmetric (i.e., $R ‚à© RÀò$ need not be included in $R$, $\Id$, nor in $\bot$;
equivalently: $RÀò$ need not be included in $R, \sim R ‚à™ \Id,$ nor in $\sim R$);
e.g., the relationship /likes = { ‚ü®Louise, Martin‚ü©, ‚ü®Martin, Louise‚ü©, ‚ü®Martin,
Natalie‚ü© }/ is not symmetric ---Martin likes Matalie but Natalie does not like
Martin---.  is not antisymmetric ---Martin and Louise like each other, but they
are not the same person---, and it is not asymmetric ---since Martin and Louise
like each other.

Interestingly, every homogeneous relation /R/ may be /partitioned/ into an asymmetric
part $A = R ‚à© ‚àºRÀò$ and a symmetric part $S = R ‚à© RÀò$
---i.e., $R = A ‚à™ S$ and $A ‚à© S = ‚ä•$.

- Preorder :: Models the notion of ‚Äòinclusion‚Äô or ‚Äòat most‚Äô or ‚Äòbefore‚Äô or
  ‚Äòpredecessor of‚Äô; and so requires: /Everything is included in itself and
  inclusion is transitive./

    |   | $R$ is a preorder                               |
    | /=/ | $R$ is transitive and reflexive                 |
    | /=/ | $R ‚®æ R ‚äÜ R \;‚àß\; \Id ‚äÜ R$                       |
    | /=/ | $R ‚®æ R = R \;‚àß\; \Id ‚äÜ R$                       |
    | /=/ | $R ‚ï± R = R$  ---‚Äúindirect inclusion from above‚Äù |
    | /=/ | $R ‚ï≤ R = R$  ---‚Äúindirect inclusion from below‚Äù |

    If it is additionally /antisymmetric/, one says we have an *order*.
    - The relation $R ‚à© RÀò$ is the greatest equivalence contained in a preorder $R$.
    :Proof:
    It's clearly symmetric and reflexive, and transitive since ‚®æ sub-distributes
    over ‚à© and R and RÀò are transitive. Then, for any equivalence Œû ‚äÜ R,
    we have Œû = Œû Àò ‚äÜ R Àò and so Œû ‚äÜ R ‚à© RÀò.
    :End:

   Instead of reflexivity, if we have irreflexivity we get *strict order*:
   |   | $R$ is a strict order             |
   | /=/ | $R$ is transitive and irreflexive |
   | /=/ | $R ‚®æ R ‚äÜ R ‚äÜ \sim\Id$             |
   | /=/ | $R ‚®æ R ‚äÜ R \;‚àß\; RÀò ‚äÜ \sim R$     |
   | /=/ | $R ‚®æ R ‚äÜ R \;‚àß\; R ‚à© RÀò ‚äÜ ‚ä•$      |
   | /=/ | $R$ is transitive and asymmetric  |

   ( /Warning! A ‚Äústrict order‚Äù is not an order that is somehow strict. )

   To prove these, use SchroÃàder's rule to obtain
   $R ‚®æ R ‚äÜ \sim\Id \;‚â°\; RÀò‚®æ\Id ‚äÜ \sim R$.

   + Orders and strict orders come in pairs: Every order $R$ induces a strict
     order $R ‚à© \sim\Id$; conversely, every strict order $R$ gives rise to an
     order $R ‚à™ \Id$. As such, it is customary to denote order relations by
     symbols such as ‚â§, ‚äÜ. ‚âº, ‚äë and their associated strict orders by related
     symbols <, ‚äÇ, ‚â∫, ‚äè, respectively, with *lack the horizontal line ‚Äò‚îÄ‚Äô below
     the symbol to indicate irreflexivity ---i.e., the line is a suggestive
     reminder of equality.

   + When letters are used to denote orders, one may see /E/ for an order since it
     is reminiscent of ‚â§ and ‚äÜ, and may see /C/ for a strict order since it is
     reminiscent of < and ‚äÇ.

- Equivalence :: Models the notion of ‚Äòsimilarity‚Äô; /Everything is similar to
  itself, being similar is a mutual relationship, and it is transitive/.

    |   | $R$ is an equivalence                         |
    | /=/ | $R$ is a symmetric preorder                   |
    | /=/ | $R$ is transitive and reflexive and symmetric |
    | /=/ | $R ‚®æ R ‚äÜ R \;‚àß\; \Id ‚äÜ R ‚äÜ RÀò$                |
    | /=/ | $R ‚®æ R = R = RÀò \;‚àß\; \Id ‚äÜ R$                |
    | /=/ | $R ‚®æ R Àò ‚äÜ R \;‚àß\; \Id ‚äÜ R$                   |

    Its Boolean matrix has block diagonal form /if/ suitably arranged.

- Linear :: /Any two (possibly identical) members are related/; (the associated
  graph can be drawn /similar/ to a line; i.e., the nodes can be arranged in a
  sequence).

  ( In graph terminology, linear is also referred to as /strongly complete/. )

  ( Sometimes a linear /order/ is called a /complete order/. )

  |   | $R$ is linear                    |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢ x „ÄîR„Äï y  ‚à®  y „ÄîR„Äï x/ |
  | /=/ | $‚ä§ ‚äÜ R ‚à™ R Àò$                    |
  | /=/ | $\sim R ‚äÜ R Àò$                   |
  | /=/ | $\sim R$ is asymmetric           |

  A linear /order/ corresponds to a full upper triangular matrix, /after/ suitably
  arranging rows and columns. A linear (pre)-/order/ has no (distinct)
  incomparable elements.

  Any linear ordering /E/, with associated strict order /C/, satisfies $CÀò = \sim
  E$; i.e., any linear order ‚Äò‚äë‚Äô satisfies \[ ‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢\quad ¬¨ (x ‚äë y) \;‚â°\; y ‚äè
  x \]

  Likewise, for liner order, we have /transitivity E‚®æC‚®æE = C/ and /weakening C ‚äÜ E/;
  i.e.,
  \[ a ‚äë b ‚äè c ‚äë d \;‚áí\; a ‚äè d \quad\text{ and }\quad x ‚äè y \;‚áí\; x ‚äë y \]

  Every order /E/ can be extended to a linear order /E‚Ä≤/; i.e., /E ‚äÜ E‚Ä≤/.
  For the finite case this is known as /topological sort/, covered later on,
  and for the infinite case this is known as the /Szpilrajn extension/.
  - For the finite case, the /idea/ is as follows: If /E/ is not linear, then there
    are two incomparable elements /x, y/ (i.e., outside /E ‚à™ EÀò/), so we may define
    /an/ ordering /E‚ÇÅ ‚âî E ‚à™ {(x, y)}/. We iterate this process and /E‚Çô/ will
    eventually become linear.

    This process maintains ‚Äúthe order /E/, less the incomparable elements, is
    linear‚Äù invariant throughout. Since each step reduces the number of
    incomparable elements, it must terminate, and the invariant then ensures the
    resulting order is linear. (‚Ä¢ÃÄ·¥ó‚Ä¢ÃÅ)Ÿà

- Semilinear :: /Any two different members are related/; (the associated graph can be drawn
  similar to a line).

  ( In graph terminology, semilinear is also referred to as /complete/; e.g., /‚Äúthe
  complete graph on n nodes‚Äù/ refers to $‚ä§ ‚à© ‚àº\Id : 1..n ‚Üî 1..n$. )

  |   | $R$ is semilinear                          |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢ x ‚â† y  ‚áí  x „ÄîR„Äï y  ‚à®  y „ÄîR„Äï x/ |
  | /=/ | $\sim\Id ‚äÜ R ‚à™ R Àò$                        |
  | /=/ | $\sim R ‚äÜ R Àò ‚à™ \Id$                       |
  | /=/ | $\sim R$ is antisymmetric                  |

  A relation without incomparable elements is semilinear.

  A semilinear and asymmetric relation $R$ is known as a /tournament/ since it
  models the win-loss situation of a typical sports tournament: Semilinearity
  and asymmetry ensure teams do not play against themselves and that there is no
  draw ---i.e., there must be a winner. A tournament /R/ is characterised by /R ‚à™
  RÀò = ‚àºId/.

** Properties of /Heterogeneous/ Relations
   :PROPERTIES:
   :CUSTOM_ID: COMMENT-Properties-of-Heterogeneous-Relations
   :END:

- *Univalent (partially defined function):* /Equal elements are related to equal elements; i.e., an element
  cannot be related to two different elements./

  /That is, every source value x is associated *at most one* target value y./

  As a simple graph: /Any arcs from the same source actually coincide./

  |   | $R$ is univalent                                                          |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ x, y, y‚Ä≤  ‚Ä¢ x „Äî R „Äï y ‚àß x „ÄîR„Äï y‚Ä≤  ‚áí y = y‚Ä≤/                          |
  | /=/ | $R Àò ‚®æ R  ‚äÜ \Id$                                                          |
  | /=/ | $R ‚®æ \sim \Id \;‚äÜ\; \sim R$                                               |
  | /=/  | $‚àÄ S ‚Ä¢ R ‚®æ \sim S \;‚äÜ\; \sim (R ‚®æ S)$                                      |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ S ‚Ä¢ R ‚®æ ‚àº S = R ‚®æ ‚ä§ ‚à© ‚àº(R ‚®æ S)/                                          |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ Q, S ‚Ä¢  R ‚®æ (Q ‚à© S) = R ‚®æ Q ‚à© R ‚®æ S/   ---c.f., ‚®æ sub-distributes over ‚à© |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ Q, S ‚Ä¢ Q‚®æR ‚à© S = (Q ‚à© S ‚®æ RÀò)‚®æR/       ---c.f., the Dedekind rule        |

  The formula $R ‚®æ \sim \Id \;‚äÜ \sim R$ reads ‚ÄúIf /x/ is /R/-related to a value
  different from /y/, then it is not /R/-related to /y/.‚Äù
  It continues to hold when we replace the identity by an arbitrary relation.

  The 5th row reads, /the preimage of the complement is the same as the
  complement of the preimage intersected with the domain/.  In fact, for
  univalent $R$, we also have $\sim(R ‚®æ S) = R ‚®æ \sim S ‚à™ \sim(R ‚®æ ‚ä§)$; e.g.,
  the people who do ‚Äúnot (own an Audi car)‚Äù are exactly the people who ‚Äú(own a
  non-Audi car) or do not(own any car)‚Äù ---assuming a person can own at most one
  car.

  For a map $f$, the 6th row becomes: $f(A ‚à© B) \;=\; f(A) ‚à© f(B)$,
  using conventional direct image notation; i.e.,
  /for a function, the preimage of an intersection is the intersection of preimages/.

  Likewise, for a map $f$, we have /the intersection of $B$ with a function's/
  /image is the same as the image of an intersection involving the preimage of
  $B$/; i.e., $f(A) ‚à© B = f(A ‚à© f^{-1}(B))$.

  # |   | /Q ‚®æ R ‚à© S (determine the R's of Q's image *and* extract those that are in S)/             |
  # | /=/ | /(S ‚®æ RÀò ‚à© Q)‚®æR (determine the co-R's from S's that are Q, *then* relate them  to their R's)/ |

- *Total:* /Every source value x is associated *at least one* target value y./

  |   | $R$ is total                          |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ x ‚Ä¢ ‚àÉ y ‚Ä¢ x „Äî R „Äï y/               |
  | /=/ | $‚ä§ = R ‚®æ ‚ä§$ (‚Äúdefined everywhere‚Äù)    |
  | /=/ | $‚ä• = \sim (R ‚®æ ‚ä§)$                    |
  | /=/ | $\Id ‚äÜ R ‚®æ R Àò$                       |
  | /=/ | $\sim R \;‚äÜ\; R ‚®æ \sim \Id$           |
  | /=/ | $‚àÄ S ‚Ä¢ \sim (R ‚®æ S) \;‚äÜ\; R ‚®æ \sim S$ |
  | /=/ | $‚àÄ Q ‚Ä¢ Q ‚®æ R = ‚ä• ‚â° Q = ‚ä•$             |

  The formula $\sim R \;‚äÜ\; R ‚®æ \sim \Id$ reads ‚ÄúIf /x/ is not /R/-related to
  /y/, then /x/ is /R/related to some element different from /y/.‚Äù
  It continues to hold when we replace the identity by an arbitrary relation.

  The final formula says that $R$ is post-annihilated by the empty relation only.

  + Note: $\sim(R ‚®æ ‚ä§) = ‚ä§ \;‚â°\; R = ‚ä•$, for any $R$.

    /The complement of a relation's domain is everything precisely when the
    relation is empty./

- *Map (totally defined function):* /Every source value x is associated *exactly one*
  target value y./

  |   | $F$ is a map                          |
  | /=/ | $F$ is total and univalent            |
  | /=/ | $F ‚®æ \sim \Id \;=\; \sim F$           |
  | /=/ | $‚àÄ S ‚Ä¢ F ‚®æ \sim S \;=\; \sim (F ‚®æ S)$ |

  The final rule says /the preimage of the complement is the complement of the
  preimage/; or, using conventional direct image notation, $f‚Åª¬π(\sim A) \;=\;
  \sim f‚Åª¬π(A)$.

  In conventional direct image notation, this amount to a Galois connection:
  $A ‚äÜ f‚Åª¬π(B) \quad‚â°\quad f(A) ‚äÜ B$.

  A mapping is so very close to being invertible since mappings $F$ always
  satisfy: $F Àò ‚®æ F ‚äÜ \Id$ and $\Id ‚äÜ F ‚®æ FÀò$.

  *Shunting rule:* If $F$ is a map, then
  \[R ‚äÜ S ‚®æ F Àò \quad‚â°\quad R ‚®æ F ‚äÜ S \]

  More generally, given an equivalence Œû, if relation /F/ is total and Œû-univalent
  ---i.e., /FÀò ‚®æ F ‚äÜ Œû/--- and if /S/ is Œû-target-saturated ---i.e., /S ‚®æ Œû = S/---
  then $R ‚äÜ S ‚®æ F Àò \quad‚â°\quad R ‚®æ F ‚äÜ S$.

- *Surjective:* /Every source value y is associated *at least* one source value x./

  |   | $R$ is surjective           |
  | /=/ | $RÀò$ is total               |
  | /=/ | $‚ä§ ‚®æ R = ‚ä§$                 |
  | /=/ | $\Id ‚äÜ R Àò ‚®æ R$             |
  | /=/ | $\sim R \;‚äÜ\; \sim \Id ‚®æ R$ |
  | /=/ | /‚àÄ S ‚Ä¢ R ‚®æ S = ‚ä• ‚â° S = ‚ä•/   |

- *Injective:* /Every source value y is associated *at most* one source value x./

  |   | $R$ is injective            |
  | /=/ | $RÀò$ is univalent           |
  | /=/ | $R  ‚®æ R Àò ‚äÜ \Id$            |
  | /=/ | $\sim \Id ‚®æ R \;‚äÜ\; \sim R$ |

- *Bijective:* /Every source value y is associated *exactly one* source value x./

  |   | $R$ is bijective                |
  | /=/ | $R$ is injective and surjective |

- An *iso* is a bijective mapping, also known as a *permutation.*

- If $R$ is finite, then
  \[ R ‚®æ R Àò = \Id \quad‚â°\quad  (‚àÉ m ‚Ä¢ R·µê = \Id ‚àß R·µê‚Åª¬π = R Àò) \]

  For the nontrivial direction ‚Äú‚áí‚Äù, since $X$ and $Y$ are finite
  that means $X ‚Üî Y$ is finite and so among all powers of $R$,
  at least two coincide ---by the pigeon-hole principle.
  Say, $R‚Å± = R ≤$ for some $i > j$. Then

  #+begin_calc
  + R‚Å±‚Åª ≤
  + R‚Å± ‚®æ (R Àò) ≤ -- Negative powers as powers of converse
  + R‚Å± ‚®æ (R Àò)‚Å± -- Assumption $R‚Å± \,=\, R ≤$
  + \Id -- $R ‚®æ RÀò \,=\, \Id$ and so, apply induction
  #+end_calc

  Hence, $R·µê = \Id$ where $m = i - j$.

  Then, $R·µê‚Åª¬π = R·µê‚Åª¬π ‚®æ \Id = R·µê‚Åª¬π ‚®æ R ‚®æ R Àò = R·µê ‚®æ RÀò = \Id ‚®æ RÀò = RÀò$ ‚ô•‚Äø‚ô•

- Difunctional :: This property generalises injectivity, univalence, and equivalence...

  Recall,
  - Univalent: Every source value /x/ is associated *at most one* target value /y/.
    + I.e., if /x/ goes to /y/ and /y‚Ä≤/ then /y = y‚Ä≤/.
    + I.e., $‚àÄ x, y‚Ä≤, y ‚Ä¢\quad  x „ÄîR„Äï y  „ÄîRÀò„Äï x „ÄîR„Äï y‚Ä≤ \;‚áí\; y „Äî\Id„Äï y‚Ä≤$
  - Injective: Every source value /y/ is associated *at most* one source value /x/.
    + I.e., if /y/ comes from /x/ and /x‚Ä≤/ then /x = x‚Ä≤/.
    + I.e., $‚àÄ x, x‚Ä≤, y ‚Ä¢\quad  x „ÄîR„Äï y  „ÄîRÀò„Äï x‚Ä≤ „ÄîR„Äï y \;‚áí\; x „Äî\Id„Äï x‚Ä≤$
  - Equivalence: Any given equivalence classes are either identical or disjoint.
      # + I.e., $‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢\quad  x „ÄîR„Äï y  „ÄîRÀò„Äï x „ÄîR„Äï y‚Ä≤ \;‚áí\; x „ÄîR„Äï y‚Ä≤$
    + Moreover, it is a /homogenous/ relation.

  Now, a /possibly heterogenous/ relation /R/ is /difunctional/ exactly when
  \[ ‚àÄ x, x‚Ä≤, y‚Ä≤, y ‚Ä¢\quad  x „ÄîR„Äï y  „ÄîRÀò„Äï x‚Ä≤ „ÄîR„Äï y‚Ä≤ \;‚áí\; x „ÄîR„Äï y‚Ä≤ \]
  That is, $R ‚®æ R Àò ‚®æ R ‚äÜ R$; in-fact we have equality $R ‚®æ R Àò ‚®æ R = R$.
  Using SchroÃàder, this amounts to $R ‚®æ ‚àºR Àò ‚®æ R \;‚äÜ\; ‚àºR$.

  Clearly, converse preserves difunctionality.

  For difunctional /R/,
  1. /R ‚®æ (Q ‚à© RÀò ‚®æ S) = R ‚®æ Q ‚à© R ‚®æ RÀò ‚®æ S/
  2. $R ‚®æ ‚àº(R Àò ‚®æ Q) \;=\; R ‚®æ ‚ä§ ‚à© ‚àº(R ‚®æ RÀò Q)$
  3. $‚àº(R ‚®æ R Àò ‚®æ Q) \;=\; R ‚®æ ‚àº(RÀò ‚®æ Q) ‚à™ ‚àº(R ‚®æ ‚ä§)$
  4. $R ‚®æ ‚àº(R Àò ‚®æ Q) \;=\; ‚àº(R ‚®æ RÀò Q)$, if /R/ is also total.

 The equivalence target-saturation of a univalent relation is difunctional; i.e.,
 if /R/ is univalent and Œû is an equivalence, then $R ‚®æ Œû$ is difunctional.

** Equivalence Relations
   :PROPERTIES:
   :CUSTOM_ID: Equivalence-Relations
   :END:

Relations $R$ that /model/
‚Äòequality, similarity, indistinguishability‚Äô are known as *equivalence relations* and they
must satisfy the following properties which we expect of the notion of
‚Äòsimilarity‚Äô:
- *Reflexive:* /Everything is similar to itself/

  \[‚àÄ x ‚Ä¢\; x „Äî R„Äï x \]

- *Symmetric:* /Being similar is a mutual relationship; if one thing is similar to
  the other, then the other should be similar to the first./

  \[‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢\; x „Äî R „Äï y ‚áí y „Äî R„Äï x\]

- *Transitive:* /Things similar to things that are similar, are themselves similar./

  \[‚àÄ x, y, z ‚Ä¢ \; x „Äî R „Äï y „ÄîR „Äï z \,‚áí\, x „ÄîR„Äï z\]

For example, ‚Äú2 + 3‚Äù and ‚Äú5‚Äù are clearly *not the same*‚Äù: The first is a string
of 3 symbols, whereas the latter is a string of a single symbol.  However, they
are *equivalent* when we evaluate them and so we want to pretend they are the
same, not by using equality, but by using an equivalence relation.  ( This
example is continued when we discuss transitive closures. )

These kinds of relations occur all the time in real life.  E.g., the relation
‚Äúsame zodiac sign‚Äù is an equivalence relation.  As such, ‚ÄúI'm looking for a Leo‚Äù
means /anyone/ who has the Leo zodiac sign is ‚Äúgood enough‚Äù ---all such people are
essentially ‚Äòindefatigable‚Äô since their distinguishing feature, at the moment,
is their zodiac sing.

Generalising the previous two examples, ‚Äúsharing the same feature ùíá‚Äù is an
equivalence relation.  That is, if $f : A ‚Üí B$ is a function, then $\sim_f$ is
an equivalence relation defined by $a‚ÇÅ \sim_f a‚ÇÇ \;‚â°\; f(a‚ÇÅ) = f(a‚ÇÇ)$. In
databases, one uses essentially this idea: ‚ÄúTwo tables are the same if the have
the same primary keys.‚Äù
#+begin_latex-definitions
\def\ker{\mathsf{ker}\,}
#+end_latex-definitions
#+begin_box Kernels
Generalising the above $‚àº_f$ for function $f$, one can define the (possibly non-equivalence)
relation ‚Äúindistinguishable by $R$‚Äù $\ker R \;=\; R Àò ‚®æ R$ ---also known as ‚Äúthe
kernel of R‚Äù. Two elements are related by the kernel of /R/ exactly when /R/
associates them with the same thing; i.e., they ‚Äòcollide‚Äô on a same target
value.  \[ a „Äî\ker R „Äï a‚Ä≤ \quad‚â°\quad (‚àÉ b ‚Ä¢ a „ÄîR„Äï b \;‚àß\; a‚Ä≤ „ÄîR„Äï b) \]
The kernel of /R/ measures the /injectivity/ of /R/: The larger it is the larger the
set of inputs which /R/ is unable to distinguish ---i.e., the less /injective/ $R$
is.
+ /R injective ‚â° ker R ‚äÜ Id/
+ /R total    ‚â° Id ‚äÜ ker R/

See [[https://reader.elsevier.com/reader/sd/pii/S1567832614000149?token=2DC8684CD3C11B11A0E970A94963D27B2518A228A367766E39D79D13D9A1B8D83EBA7ED1460D30A49DFB1C13956D638C][here ]] for more.
#+end_box

Modulo Arithmetic captures the idea that numbers
‚Äúare equal‚Äù if we consider not a ‚Äònumber line‚Äô but
instead a ‚Äònumber circle‚Äô ---e.g., on a usual clock,
24 O'clock is the same as 12 PM, and 13 O'clock is
the same as 1PM; likewise, 48 O'clock lands on the same
spot on the clock face as does 24 and 12 and 0.

*Modulo equivalence:* $a =_m b  \;‚â°\;  (‚àÉ k : ‚Ñ§ ‚Ä¢ a - b = m ¬∑ k)$.

( Notice that $a =_m b \;‚â°\; m ‚à£ (a - b)$;
one says ‚Äú$a$ is the same as $b$ modulo $m$‚Äù. )

# The following relation was initially studied by Gauss.
# Much of Number Theory is devoted to the study of this equivalence
# relation and its application in various things we use daily.
#

#+begin_box Aside: Characterising Equivalences with ‚ÄúIndirect Equivalence‚Äù
| Œû is an equivalence | ‚â° | $‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢  x „ÄîŒû„Äï y \quad‚â°\quad (‚àÄ z ‚Ä¢ x „ÄîŒû„Äï z \;‚â°\; y „ÄîŒû„Äï z)$ |
#+end_box

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

The required properties of an equivalence relation are /independent/: None
imply any other. E.g., define $R : ‚Ñ§ ‚Üî ‚Ñ§$ by /a „Äî R „Äïb ‚â° a ¬∑ b >= 0/,
then this is a reflexive and symmetric relation, but is not transitive:
$-2 „ÄîR„Äï 0 „ÄîR„Äï 7$ but we do not have $-2 „ÄîR„Äï 7$.

Likewise, many orders are reflexive and transitive, but not symmetric.
E.g., divisibility ‚Äò‚à£‚Äô.

Likewise, ‚Äúbeing 4 units apart‚Äù is reflexive and symmetric, but not transitive.
---where $a „Äî R„Äï b \;‚â°\; |a - b| ‚â§ 4$.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

# Let Œû be an equivalence relation ---the name has been chosen since it looks like equality, =, and equivalence, ‚â°.

Let $Œû : A ‚Üî A$ be an equivalence relation, given any value $x$, let \[ [x]_Œû \;=\; \{ y \;‚ùô\; x
„Äî Œû „Äï y \} \] This set is known as an *equivalence class (Œû-class)* of the
equivalence relation Œû determined by $x$.

That is, $[x]_Œû$ is the set of all things that are ‚ÄòŒû-indistinguishable‚Äô from $x$.

#+begin_details Quotient Construction
*The quotient of $A$ modulo Œû* is the set of all equivalence classes; it is
usually denoted $A/Œû$ ---which we will avoid since it clashes with residuals---
or $A \mod Œû$ ---in analogy with modulo arithmetic, and then one also sees $[x]_Œû$
denoted $x \mod Œû$.
For example, simple graphs ---i.e., those without parallel edges---
are obtained as the quotient of total graphs over Œû being edge-parallelism
---details are below in the section on graphs.

The /mapping/ $Œ∑ : x ‚Ü¶ [x]_Œû$ is called the *natural projection*; its type is $A ‚Üí A
\mod Œû$ and it is characterised (up to isomorphism) as the unique mapping Œ∑
satisfying \[ Œû = Œ∑‚®æŒ∑Àò \qquad\text{ and }\qquad Œ∑Àò ‚®æ Œ∑ = \Id \] @remark: If
there's any other such mapping Œ∑‚Ä≤, then Œ∑‚Ä≤Àò ‚®æ Œ∑ is a bijection between their
targets.@@ In general, terms starting from a quotient set begin with $Œ∑Àò$ and
terms ending in a quotient set end with $Œ∑$ ---the former gives us a
/representative/ to work with, and the latter yields an equivalence class which is
a value of the quotient.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Whenever $a „ÄîŒû„Äïa‚Ä≤$, we have /multiple names denoting the same thing/, namely
both $Œ∑(a)$ and $Œ∑(a‚Ä≤)$ denote the same equivalence class.  More concretely, in
modular arithmetic, $1 \mod 4$ and $ 401 \mod 4$ are /two different ways/ to
/denote the same equivalence class/; so when we define a function /by considering
representatives/ such as $\even([x]_{=_4}) = 2 ‚à£ x$ we need to make sure that it
doesn't depend on the /representative x/.  That is, when defining functions $f :
(A \mod Œû) ‚Üí B$ with an element of quotient set as an argument, one must show
that the function definitions are ‚Äúindependent of the choice of representative‚Äù:
$‚àÄ a, a‚Ä≤ ‚Ä¢ a „ÄîŒû„Äï a‚Ä≤ \;‚áí\; f(a) = f(a‚Ä≤)$; i.e., $Œû ‚äÜ f ‚®æ fÀò$.

In /constructive/ mathematics ---e.g., in Agda--- one uses ‚Äòsetoids, E-sets‚Äô $(A,
‚âà)$ which are sets $A$ /along with/ an equivalence relation $‚âà$ and only considers
the functions $f : (A, ‚âà) ‚Üí (A‚Ä≤, ‚âà‚Ä≤)$ that preserve these relations ---in that,
they satisfy the representative-independence condition $‚âà \;‚äÜ\; f ‚®æ ‚âà‚Ä≤ ‚®æ fÀò$,
which is the same as the above but the second ‚âà‚Ä≤ above was classical ‚Äò=‚Äô (/Id/).
Then, if one begins with the setoid $(A, ‚âà)$ and Œû is an equivalence with
respect to ‚âà ---not the strict ‚Äò=‚Äô--- then $A \mod Œû \;=\; (A, Œû)$ and $Œ∑(x) =
x$.  Indeed, that Œ∑ is representative-independent then is precisely the
requirement that Œû is ‚âà-reflexive.

Congruences are touched on in the next box ;-)

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

It is argued that setoids are how we /usually/ think, after all membership is the
cornerstone of set theory and it depends on the particular equivalence relation
‚Äò=‚Äô, which is why we require quotient constructions. On the other hand, with
setoids there is no new set being produced but only a change in what it means to
be ‚Äòequal‚Äô.
#+end_details

#+begin_details Congruences: Relational structure respecting equivalences
As will be discussed below, and already alluded to in the previous ‚Äòdetails‚Äô
box, equivalences lead to a partitioning into classes which are nicer to work
with since there are fewer in number than the starting set ---e.g., for
implementation purposes.

When is it possible that the operations we want to use actually behave nicely
with respect to the subdivision into classes? E.g., number addition continues to
work nicely when we use it with same-remainder-classes, $‚Ñ§ \,\mathsf{mod}\, =‚Çò$.

Suppose $(X, Œû)$ and $(X‚Ä≤, Œû‚Ä≤)$ are sets endowed with equivalence relations.
We say *Œû and Œû‚Ä≤ are an /R/-congruence* when $Œû ‚®æ R ‚äÜ R ‚®æ Œû‚Ä≤$; i.e.,
when the Œû-source-saturation of /R/ is included in the Œû‚Ä≤-target-saturation
---which is a super nice way of saying:
/Whenever x „ÄîR„Äï y, then the image of the Œû-equivalence class of x
is contained in the image of the Œû‚Ä≤-equivalence class of y/;
--or, more loosely,
/if R relates representatives then it relates equivalence classes/.

$\def\fst{\mathsf{fst}}$ For example, let $\fst : X √ó Y ‚Üî X$ denote the
projection to the first component, Œû be ‚Äúhave common first argument‚Äù ---i.e., /Œû
= fst ‚®æ fstÀò/--- and Œû‚Ä≤ be the identity. Then (Œû, Œû‚Ä≤) constitute a
/fst/-congruence.
#+end_details

By Œû-reflexivity, the Œû-classes are never empty --- $[x]_Œû ‚â† ‚àÖ$ --- and, by
Œû-symmetry and Œû-transitivity, we have that ‚Äúclass membership and class equality
coincide‚Äù --- $a ‚àà [b]_Œû ‚â° [a]_Œû = [b]_Œû$ --- and this property shows that
*equivalence relations really do capture equality ‚Äúup to a perspective by Œû‚Äù*.
The previous two properties show that the classes are disjoint --- $[a]_Œû =
[b]_Œû \;‚â¢\; [a]_Œû ‚à© [b]_Œû = ‚àÖ$ --- and the domain of discourse is covered
by all of the equivalence classes --- $A = (‚à™ a : A \;‚ùô\; [a]_Œû)$.
The last two properties together says that *the equivalence classes partition
the entire universe*: Every member of the universe belongs to exactly one class.

( Indeed, each member $x$ belongs only to class $[x]_Œû$ and indistinguishable members
belong to the same class! )

Formally, a *partition* of a non-empty set /S/ is a division of /S/ into
non-intersecting non-empty subsets; i.e., $P : ‚Ñô S$ partitions $S$ iff
$S = (‚à™ X \;‚ùô\; X ‚àà P ‚Ä¢ X)$ and $‚àÄ X, Y \;‚ùô\; X ‚àà P ‚àß Y ‚àà P ‚Ä¢  X = Y ‚â¢ X ‚à© Y = ‚àÖ$
---whence $(‚àÄ X \;‚ùô\; X ‚àà P ‚Ä¢ X ‚â† ‚àÖ)$.

E.g., the sets of even and odd integers partition all of the integers ‚Ñ§;
likewise the negatives and the non-negatives partition ‚Ñ§; finally the singletons
$P = \{n : ‚Ñ§ \;‚ùô\; \{n\} \}$ also partition all of ‚Ñ§.  ( The last example is an
instance of equivalence classes partitioning a universe, where $[x]_Œû = \{x\}$
... Œû is the identity (equality) relation! )

An interesting example is the modular arithmetic equivalence relation.  E.g., $‚Ñ§
= [0]_{=‚ÇÉ} ‚à™ [1]_{=‚ÇÉ} ‚à™ [2]_{=‚ÇÉ}$.  More generally, $‚Ñ§ = (‚à™ i \;‚ùô\; i < m ‚Ä¢
[i]_{=‚Çò})$.

Surprisingly, *equivalence relations in fact coincide with partitions!* Indeed,
given a way ---a ‚Äòclass system‚Äô--- to split up the universe, we can declare two
members to be ‚Äòindistinguishable‚Äô precisely when they belong to the same class.
That is, if $P$ partitions $A$, then
$Œû_P$ is an equivalence relation which is defined by
$x „ÄîŒû_P„Äï y \;‚â°\; (‚àÉ C \;‚ùô\; C ‚àà P ‚Ä¢ x ‚àà C \,‚àß\, y ‚àà C)$;
moreover, the equivalence classes of $Œû_P$ are $P$!

Hence, we can /model/ ‚Äòindistinguishability‚Äô by either equivalence relations
or by partitions ---same thing, but different representations.

*Practically,* this means that to determine all the equivalence relations on a set
it suffices to determine the partitions only, which is far more straightforward
to do! E.g., to determine the equivalence relations on 1..3, we find that there
are exactly five partitions: The singletons, the entire universe, and
/{{1}, {2, 3}}/, /{{2}, {1, 3}}/, and /{{3}, {1, 2}}/. From these we compute the
associated relations.

Notice:
- If $P = \{x \;‚ùô\; \{x\}\}$ is the singletons partition, then $Œû_P = \Id$.
- If $P = \{ A \}$ is the partition consisting of the entire universe, then $Œû_P
  = ‚ä§$.

#+begin_box Surjections (‚ÄúColourings‚Äù) ‚âà Equivalences ‚âà Partitions
Surjective functions correspond precisely to equivalence relations:
\[ f \text{ surjective } \quad‚â°\quad \sim_f \text{ equivalence } \]
\[ [\_]_Œû \text{ surjective } \quad‚â°\quad Œû \text{ equivalence } \]

A surjective function, or partition, can be thought of as a
‚Äúa classification‚Äù; e.g., in graph theory,
an /n/-partite graph is a graph that can be coloured with at most /n/ colours
(in such a way that related points have distinct colours).
#+end_box

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Since transitivity is not preserved by intersections, neither are equivalence
relations. However, equivalence relations are preserved by union.

#+begin_box Theorems for equivalence relations Œû

Say a relation /R is saturated with equivalence Œû on the source side/ whenever an
element of an equivalence class is related to some element, then all are related
to that element; i.e., /x „ÄîR„Äï y ‚áí ‚àÄ x‚Ä≤ ‚Ä¢ x „ÄîŒû„Äï x‚Ä≤ ‚áí x‚Ä≤ „ÄîR„Äï y/.  For example,
in a graph, the operation sending an edge to its starting point is source
saturated with Œû being edge-parallelism ---see the section on graphs below for
details.  The saturation of a relation /R/ is /Œû ‚®æ R/; since Œû is idempotent, the
saturation of already saturated relations produces no change: /Œû ‚®æ (Œû ‚®æ R) = Œû ‚®æ
R/.  Similar results hold for target-saturated relations ---e.g., the first two
theorems below could be transposed, Àò, to provide target-saturation results
since /Œû Àò = Œû/.

1. $Œû ‚®æ \sim (Œû ‚®æ R) ‚®æ Œû \quad=\quad \sim(Œû ‚®æ R)$

   The complement of a source-saturated relation is again source-saturated.

3. $Œû ‚®æ (Œû ‚®æ Q ‚à© R)  \quad=\quad Œû ‚®æ Q ‚à© Œû ‚®æ R \quad=\quad Œû ‚®æ (Q ‚à© Œû ‚®æ R)$
   # and: $(Q ‚®æ Œû ‚à© R) ‚®æ Œû \quad=\quad Q ‚®æ Œû ‚à© R ‚®æ Œû \quad=\quad (Q ‚à© R ‚®æ Œû) ‚®æ Œû$

   The saturation of the intersection of a saturated relation with another one
   is the same as the intersection of the two saturated relations.

4. For Œ© an equivalence: $G Àò ‚®æ Œû ‚®æ G ‚äÜ Œ© \;‚àß\; F ‚®æ ‚ä§ ‚äá G ‚®æ ‚ä§ \quad‚áí\quad
   \left(F ‚®æ Œ© ‚äá G ‚®æ Œ© \;‚â°\; F ‚®æ Œ© = G ‚®æ Œ©\right)$

   That is, /If G relates Œû-equivalent elements to Œ©-equivalent elements and the/
   /domain of F contains the domain of G, then F and G are (pointwise)
   Œ©-equivalent precisely when F is (pointwise) Œ©-included in G./

   In particular, taking $Œû = Œ© = \Id$, this reads:
   /If G is univalent and the domain of F is contained in G,
   then F is included in G precisely when it is equal to G/.

   Moreover, this yields:
   *Inclusion of maps (total and univalent relations) is the same as equality of maps.*
5. If $E$ is a preorder that ‚Äòrespects‚Äô Œû ---i.e., $E ‚®æ Œû ‚äÜ Œû ‚®æ E$ and /E/ is
   ‚ÄòŒû-antisymmetric‚Äô: $E ‚®æ Œû ‚à© Œû ‚®æ E Àò ‚äÜ E$ --- then $Œû ‚®æ E ‚®æ Œû$ is a preorder
   and Œû is the largest equivalence relation contained in $Œû ‚®æ E ‚®æ Œû$ ---i.e.,
   $Œû ‚®æ E ‚®æ Œû ‚à© Œû ‚®æ E Àò ‚®æ Œû = Œû$ (see section on preorders for the general
   statement).

   In particular, the conclusion asserts that the preorder /E/ is induces a
   preorder $E_Œû = Œû ‚®æ E ‚®æ Œû$ on the equivalence classes; i.e.,
   \[ [x]_Œû „ÄîE_Œû„Äï [y]_Œû \qquad‚â°\qquad (‚àÉ l, r ‚Ä¢ x „ÄîŒû„Äï l „ÄîE„Äï r „ÄîŒû„Äï y)\]

   In particular, taking $Œû = E ‚à© EÀò$ truthifies the hypotheses of the theorem
   and so every preorder $E$ induces an ordering on the equivalence classes of
   $E ‚à© EÀò$.
6. Similar to Lattice Theory's ‚Äòmodular law‚Äô:
   $Q ‚äÜ Œû \quad‚áí\quad Q ‚®æ (R ‚à© Œû) \;=\; Q ‚®æ R ‚à© Œû$.
#+end_box

** Closures
   :PROPERTIES:
   :CUSTOM_ID: Closures
   :END:

Given an ordered set (œÑ, ‚äë),
let œÜ be any /property/; i.e., a predicate /œÑ ‚Üí ùîπ/.

Sometimes a member does not have the property œÜ, but
we can ‚Äúadd to it just enough‚Äù so that œÜ will then hold.

*Definition:* Say $\bar{q}$ is the *œÜ-closure* of $q$
precisely when $\bar{q}$ is the smallest member that includes $q$
and satisfies œÜ. Clearly, if $q$ satisfies œÜ then its closure is just itself:
$\bar{q}= q$.

- Formally, $œÜ(\bar{q}) \;‚àß\; q ‚äë \bar{q} \;‚àß\; ‚àÄ p ‚Ä¢ œÜ(p) \,‚àß\, q ‚äë p ‚áí \bar{q} ‚äë p$

  Equivalently, we have the following *characterisation*:

  \[œÜ(\bar{q}) \;‚àß\; ‚àÄ p \;‚ùô\; œÜ(p) ‚Ä¢ \bar{q} ‚äë p  ‚â°  q ‚äë p\]

- $\bar{q} \;=\; (‚äì p \;‚ùô\; q ‚äë p \,‚àß\, œÜ(p))$, provided both sides exist.

  This *reads like the definition*:
  | /The closure of q is the minimal element containing q and satisfying œÜ!/ |

  Notice that this meet/infimum has a /descriptive/ character as it is the meet of
  /all/ members in question, but it does not offer a /constructive/ formulation
  and indeed sometimes closures simply do not exist.

- The œÜ-closure of a value is the result of ‚Äòmaking it increasingly more Œ¶‚Äô.

#+begin_box Closure Operations
When a œÜ-closure $\bar{q}$ exists for every $q$, we have a
/closure operation/ $c(x) = \bar{x}$.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

In general, an operation $c$ between ordered spaces is known as a *closure*
if it captures the idea of ‚Äúadding just enough (to make a certain property hold,
namely the property $œÜ\, x \;‚â°\; x = c\, x$)‚Äù; i.e.,
1. /Extensive/: $x ‚äë c\, x$

   /(If we ‚Äòadd enough‚Äô, then we should contain whatever we started with.)/

2. /Monotonic:/ $x ‚äë y \;‚áí\; c\, x ‚äë c\, y$

   /(If we increase what we started with, then we need to increase how much needs
   to be ‚Äòadded‚Äô.)/

3. /Idempotent:/ $c\, (c\, x) = c\, x$.

   /(If we've already added ‚Äòenough‚Äô, there's nothing new to add.)/

Using $œÜ\, x \;‚â°\; x = c\, x$ and $\bar{x} = c\, x$, the above œÜ-closure
characterisation gives us a nifty way to characterise closure operations in one
formula: \[ ‚àÄ x, y ‚Ä¢ c\, x ‚äë y \;‚â°\; c\, x ‚äë c\, y \]

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Below are examples of œÜ-closures that have explicit descriptions
as closure operators.
#+end_box

For example, the reflexive closure of a relation $R$ is obtained
‚Äúby adding in just enough elements to make the result reflexive‚Äù;
i.e., the closure is $\Id ‚à™ R$.
- Indeed, a relation is reflexive precisely when $\Id ‚äÜ R$,
  but inclusions can be expressed as unions and so this is the same as
  $\Id ‚à™ R = R$! Super simple fun stuff!

For example, the symmetric closure of a relation $R$ is obtained
‚Äúby adding in just enough elements to make the result symmetric‚Äù;
i.e., the closure is $RÀò ‚à™ R$.
- Same reasoning as reflexive closure.

Now, most interestingly, the transitive closure.  A relation $S$ is transitive
whenever a path can be shortened (but non-empty), $S ‚®æ S ‚äÜ S$. As such, for a
relation $S$ to be the transitive closure of $R$, we need $R ‚äÜ S$ and $S ‚®æ S ‚äÜ
S$, but combining these two means we need to have $R ‚®æ R ‚äÜ S$, hence we need to
have $R^1 ‚äÜ S$ and $R¬≤ ‚äÜ S$, but this means we have $R¬≥ = R ‚®æ R¬≤ ‚äÜ S ‚®æ S ‚äÜ S$,
and in general, (by induction), $R‚Åø‚Å∫¬π ‚äÜ S$ for all $n : ‚Ñï$. Indeed, the
transitive closure of $R$ is the relation \[R‚Å∫ \;=\; (‚à™ n : ‚Ñï \;‚ùô\; R‚Åø‚Å∫¬π) \]

Likewise, the *reflexive transitive closure* of $R$ is the relation
\[R^* \;=\; Id ‚à™ R‚Å∫ \;=\; (‚à™ n : ‚Ñï \;‚ùô\; R‚Åø) \]

( This union, suprema, exists since relations form a complete Boolean lattice. )

When $a „ÄîR‚Åø„Äï b$, one says /there is a path of length n from ‚Äòa‚Äô to ‚Äòb‚Äô/.

Even though $R^*$ is defined by an infinite union, when the relation is over a
finite set, then a finite union suffices. Intuitively, if there are only $m =
‚ôØA$ nodes, then any path of greater length must visit one of the nodes at least
twice, that is the path looks like $a „ÄîR‚Å±„Äï c „ÄîR ≤„Äï c „ÄîR·µè„Äï b$, but we can
just ‚Äòcut out‚Äô the redundant circling around ‚Äòc‚Äô to obtain the path $a „ÄîR‚Å±„Äï c
„ÄîR·µè„Äï b$. Now if this path is still greater than $m$, then there is a further
circle to cut out and we repeat the process; otherwise, we are done.
That is,
\[ R^* \;=\; (‚à™ n : ‚Ñï \;‚ùô\; n ‚â§ ‚ôØA ‚Ä¢ R‚Åø) \text{ provided } R : A ‚Üî A \text { and $A$
is finite }\]

The same pigeon-hole reasoning lets us conclude: If /R/ is a finite relation on /n/
elements, then /R‚Åø ‚äÜ (Id ‚à™ R)‚Åø‚Åª¬π/.

Finally, we may get an equivalence relation from an arbitrary relation!  The
*equivalence closure* of a relation $R$ is the smallest equivalence that contains
$R$, and this happens to be the symmetric reflexive transitive closure $(R ‚à™
R)^*$ ---i.e., we throw in all the closures of the properties required of an
equivalence relation.

#+begin_box Equivalence by Reachability
The construct $R^*$ is clearly a preorder, but $Œû(R) \;=\; (R ‚®æ RÀò)^*$ is an
equivalence relation known as *the equivalence generated by common results*.  (
Likewise $(R Àò ‚®æ R)^*$ is the equivalence generated by common arguments. )

Recall from our discussion on equivalence relations that ~‚Äú2 + 3‚Äù~ and ~‚Äú5‚Äù~ are
/clearly not equal/: The first is a string of 3 symbols whereas the latter is a
string of a single symbol. However, if we /evaluate enough/ then they will both be
the same string, namely ~‚Äú5‚Äù~. That is, we say the first two are *equal since they
have indistinguishable normal forms* (i.e., they become the same thing when there
are no more possible evaluations to perform).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Since $R^*$ is a preorder the largest equivalence contained in it is $R^* ‚à©
R^*Àò$ which, treating $R$ as a graph, partitions $R$ into its *strongly connected
components*.
#+end_box

#+html: <p>

#+begin_box The type and some theorems
$\_{}^* : (A ‚Üî B) ‚Üí (A ‚Üî B)$

1. $R^+ = (‚à© H \,‚ùô\, R ‚äÜ H \,‚àß\, R ‚®æ H ‚äÜ H)$
2. $R^+ = (‚à© H \,‚ùô\, R ‚à™ R ‚®æ H ‚äÜ H) = (‚à© H \,‚ùô\, R ‚à™ R ‚®æ H = H)$
3. $R^* ‚®æ S = (‚à© H \,‚ùô\, S ‚à™ R ‚®æ H ‚äÜ H)$
4. $R$ is transitive iff $R^+ ‚äÜ R$ iff $R^+ = R$
5. $R^+ = R ‚®æ R^*$
6. $R^* = \Id ‚à™ R^+$
7. $(R^*) Àò = (RÀò)^*$ and $(R^+) Àò = (RÀò)^+$
8. $(R ‚à™ S)^* = (R^* ‚®æ S)^* ‚®æ R^*$ and $(R ‚à™ S)^+ = R^+ ‚à™ (R^* ‚®æ S)^+ ‚®æ R^*$
9. $R^* ‚®æ S^* ‚äÜ (R ‚à™ S)^*$
#+end_box
** COMMENT Kernels
   :PROPERTIES:
   :CUSTOM_ID: Kernels
   :END:

Dual to closures are kernels...

Given an ordered set (œÑ, ‚äë),
let œÜ be any /property/; i.e., a predicate /œÑ ‚Üí ùîπ/.

Sometimes a member does not have the property œÜ, but
we would like to know ‚Äúthe largest part of it‚Äù that
does satisfy œÜ.

*Definition:* Say $\underline{q}$ is the *œÜ-kernel* of $q$
precisely when $\underline{q}$ is the largest part of $q$
satisfying œÜ. Clearly, if $q$ satisfies œÜ then its kernel is just itself:
$\underline{q}= q$.

- Formally, $œÜ(\underline{q}) \;‚àß\; \underline{q} ‚äë q \;‚àß\; ‚àÄ p ‚Ä¢ œÜ(p) \,‚àß\, p ‚äë
  q ‚áí p ‚äë \underline{q}$

  Equivalently, we have the following *characterisation*:

  \[œÜ(\underline{q}) \;‚àß\; ‚àÄ p \;‚ùô\; œÜ(p) ‚Ä¢ p ‚äë \underline{q}  ‚â°  p ‚äë q\]

- $\underline{q} \;=\; (‚äî p \;‚ùô\; p ‚äë q \,‚àß\, œÜ(p))$, provided both sides exist.

  This *reads like the definition*:
  | /The kernel of q is the maximal element contained in q and satisfying œÜ!/ |

# False.
# For example, the reflexive kernel of a relation $R$ is
# ‚Äúthe largest subpart of /R/ that is reflexive‚Äù; i.e.,
# the kernel is $\Id ‚à© R$.
# - Indeed, a relation is reflexive precisely when $\Id ‚äÜ R$, but inclusions can
#   be expressed as intersections and so this is the same as $\Id ‚à© R = R$! Super
#   simple fun stuff!

For example, the symmetric kernel of a relation $R$ is
‚Äúthe largest subpart of /R/ that is reflexive‚Äù; i.e.,
i.e., the kernel is $RÀò ‚à© R$.
- Indeed, a relation is reflexive precisely when $\Id ‚äÜ R$, but inclusions can
  be expressed as intersections and so this is the same as $\Id ‚à© R = R$! Super
  simple fun stuff!

** C‚Å¥: Interplay between composition, conversion, complement, and containment
   :PROPERTIES:
   :CUSTOM_ID: C-Interplay-between-composition-conversion-complement-and-containment
   :END:

| ‚á® *All the theorems in the boxes within this section are equivalent!* ‚á¶  |

That is, only one of them needs to be proven using quantifiers and dummy
variables, and then it can be used to prove the rest using only
relation-algebraic reasoning ---i.e., without resorting to ‚Äúunfolding ‚Äò‚äÜ‚Äô or ‚Äò‚®æ‚Äô
into quantifiers.‚Äù

Proofs not involving quantifiers are usually shorter and more transparent,
hence also easier to check and more capable of being performed by a machine.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Just as contraposition and shunting give us $p ‚àß q ‚áí r \quad‚â°\quad p ‚àß ¬¨ r ‚áí ¬¨ q$, we have
for relations:
#+begin_box SchroÃàder Equivalences
$Q ‚®æ R ‚äÜ S \quad‚áî\quad QÀò ‚®æ \sim S ‚äÜ \sim R \quad‚áî\quad \sim S ‚®æ R Àò ‚äÜ \sim Q$

That is, /we may contrapose any two relations at the cost of transposing the
third./

# The transition from one containment to another can be memorised as follows:
# Transpose the first (or second) relation and complement, and permute the other two.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

This rule has its origin /in the same paper/ as the famous De Morgan law:
‚ÄúOn the Syllogism, No. IV., and on the Logic of Relations‚Äù by Augustus De Morgan.

#+end_box

#+begin_details Flowers for SchroÃàder
Let /Q/ associate gentlemen with the ladies they are interested in,
let /R/ associate ladies with the flowers they like,
let /S/ associate gentlemen with the flowers they bought.

Then SchroÃàder's rule, $Q ‚®æ R ‚äÜ S \quad‚áî\quad QÀò ‚®æ \sim S ‚äÜ \sim R \quad‚áî\quad
\sim S ‚®æ R Àò ‚äÜ \sim Q$, says that the following three propositions are /logically
equivalent/ ---even if none are satisfied.

1. All gentlemen /g/ buy at least those flowers /f/ that are liked by some of the
   ladies interesting to them.
2. Whenever a gentleman is interested in a lady /l/, and does not buy flower /f/
   then lady /l/ is not fond of flower /f/.
3. Whenever a gentleman /g/ does not buy a flower that lady /l/ likes, then /g/ is not
   interested in /l/.

The transition between these equivalent forms is difficult to form in a natural
language such as English, but is simple using the formal SchroÃàder's rules.
#+end_details

The SchroÃàder Equivalences speak about containing a composition; in contrast, by
Dedekind, we have the following technique for showing when a composition
contains another relation.
\[Q ‚äÜ R ‚®æ S \quad‚áê\quad ‚àÉ X ‚Ä¢ X ‚äÜ S ‚àß Q ‚äÜ ‚ä§ ‚®æ X ‚àß Q ‚®æ XÀò ‚äÜ R \]

#+begin_box Dedekind Formula
$Q ‚®æ R ‚à© S \quad‚äÜ\quad (Q ‚à© S ‚®æ RÀò) ‚®æ (R ‚à© Q Àò ‚®æ S)$

This rule does not involve the complement and can be advantageous
for giving proofs in the form of a chain of containments.
#+end_box

# (\Id ‚à© R ‚®æ R Àò) ‚®æ R  =  R  =  R ‚®æ (\Id ‚à© R Àò ‚®æ R)

#+begin_details Flowers for Dedekind
Let /Q/ associate gentlemen with the ladies they are interested in,
let /R/ associate ladies with the flowers they like,
let /S/ associate gentlemen with the flowers they bought.

Then the Dedekind formula
$Q ‚®æ R ‚à© S \quad‚äÜ\quad (Q ‚à© S ‚®æ RÀò) ‚®æ (R ‚à© Q Àò ‚®æ S)$,
says the following proposition is /true/:

+ When a gentleman /g/ buys a flower /f/, which is liked by /some/ lady he is
  interested in,
  + there will be some lady /l/ that he is interested in and who likes /some/
    of the flowers he bought,
    + and, this lady /l/ will like flower /f/, that /some/ gentleman interested in her
      has bought.

The natural language formulation is, at best, convoluted to read, and the
expansion into predicate logic using ‚àÄ-‚àÉ quantifiers is so intimidatingly
complex that it may as well be useless.

The transition between these equivalent forms is difficult to form in a natural
language such as English, but is simple using the formal SchroÃàder's rules.
#+end_details

#+begin_box Solving Linear Equations ---Residuals
Given a ‚Äòmultiplication operation‚Äô, one will ask whether there exist the
quotient of one value wrt to another in the same way as, for example /12 ¬∑ n = 84/
results in /n = 7/. To cope with this question, two notions of division are needed
since relation composition is not commutative.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

$R ‚®æ X = S$ is solvable in $X$ iff $S ‚äÜ R ‚®æ (R ‚ï≤ S)$ iff $S = R ‚®æ (R ‚ï≤ S)$
where $R ‚ï≤ S = \sim(R Àò ‚®æ \sim S)$; moreover, if solutions exist, then every
solution is contained in the special solution $R ‚ï≤ S$.

That is, $R ‚®æ X = S \quad‚â°\quad X = R ‚ï≤ S$.

Proof: If $R ‚®æ X = S$ then $R ‚®æ X ‚äÜ S ‚áî R Àò ‚®æ \sim S ‚äÜ \sim X ‚áî X ‚äÜ \sim(R Àò ‚®æ
\sim S)$ by SchroÃàder and antitonicity of complement. Hence, if a solution $X$
exists, it is included in $R ‚ï≤ S$. Moreover, $S = R ‚®æ X ‚äÜ R ‚®æ (R ‚ï≤ S)$.
The reverse containment $R ‚®æ (R ‚ï≤ S) ‚äÜ S$ is, by definition of ‚Äò‚ï≤‚Äô and SchroÃàder,
always true.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

The division $R ‚ï≤ S$ is the /largest/ solution to $R ‚®æ X ‚äÜ S$ and is therefore
also the largest solution to $R ‚®æ X = S$ /if/ this equation is solvable.

Likewise, $R ‚ï± S = \sim(\sim S ‚®æ R Àò)$.
#+end_box

In the presence of complements, we can replace converse by residuals since $\sim
   R ‚ï± \sim \Id = \sim(\sim \sim \Id ‚®æ \sim R Àò) = \sim \sim R Àò = R Àò$.

#+begin_box The Middle-Exchange Rule
\[ P ‚®æ Q ‚®æ R ‚äÜ S \quad‚â°\quad P Àò ‚®æ \sim S ‚®æ R Àò ‚äÜ \sim Q \]
#+end_box

** More on Relational Division
    :PROPERTIES:
    :CUSTOM_ID: More-on-Relational-Division
    :END:

The residual $R ‚ï≤ S$ is the greatest of all relations $X$ with $R ‚®æ X ‚äÜ S$:

   \[R ‚®æ X ‚äÜ S \quad‚â°\quad X ‚äÜ R ‚ï≤ S \]

   In particular, this means we have a Galois Connection and so we have numerous
   properties, including the ‚Äòabsorption theorem‚Äô $R ‚®æ (R ‚ï≤ (R ‚®æ X)) = R ‚®æ X$.

Equivalently,
\[ a„ÄîR ‚ï≤ S„Äï c  \quad‚â°\quad (‚àÄ x ‚Ä¢ x „ÄîR„Äï a ‚áí x „ÄîS„Äï c) \]

   As matrices, this says the /a/-th column of $R$ is pointwise contained
   in the /c/-th column of $S$.

   *Reading ‚Äú‚ï≤‚Äù as an implication, then $a „ÄîR ‚ï≤ S„Äï c$ can be read as ‚Äúwhenever
   R-gets us to /a/ then S-gets us to /c/.‚Äù*
   Indeed, $a „ÄîR ‚ï≤ S„Äï c \quad‚â°\quad RÀò‚¶á\{a\}‚¶à ‚äÜ SÀò‚¶á\{c\}‚¶à$.

   As graphs, suppose we draw /R/ in red and draw /S/ in purple,
   then we draw, say, a blue arrow $a ‚ü∂ c$ precisely when
   every red predecessor of $a$ is also a purple predecessor of $c$.
   - /[[blue: a „ÄîR ‚ï≤ S„Äï c]] ¬†¬†‚â°¬†¬† (‚àÄ x ‚Ä¢ [[red: x „ÄîR„Äï a]] ¬†‚áí¬† [[purple: x „ÄîS„Äï c]])/

    ‚òÖ [[red: Every R-path to ‚Äòa‚Äô, if any,]] [[blue: has a corresponding]] [[purple: S-path
     to ‚Äòc‚Äô]] ‚òÖ

   - *We get the red, by dividing-out the blue from the purple.*

   #+begin_src dot :file "images/relational-division-as-colour-division.png"
   digraph{
   label="(Completing the triangle by)\n Dividing the purple by the red to get the blue\n(  red ‚®æ blue  ‚äÜ  purple  )"
   ‚ãØ [style=filled];
   ‚ãØ -> {c} [color=purple, style=dashed, label=S]
   ‚ãØ -> {c} [color=purple, style=dashed]
   ‚ãØ -> {c} [color=purple, style=dashed]
   ‚ãØ -> {a} [color=red, style=dotted]
   ‚ãØ -> {a} [color=red, style=dotted, label="R"]
   a -> c [color = blue, label = R‚ï≤S]
   }
   #+end_src

   #+RESULTS:
   [[file:images/relational-division-as-colour-division.png]]

   *There has to be ‚Äúmore purple than red in order to get blue‚Äù;
   in particular, when there is no red, we can still get blue.*

   That is, when *green:every* purple:purple purple:path
   could instead take a red:red red:detour, then we blue:complete the detour
   with a blue:blue road so as to end at the purple:purple purple:path;
   when there is no possible detour, we

   If we can red:ever get to a pit-stop along a purple:path, then we can
   blue:complete the journey from the pit-stop to the intended destination.


      #+begin_src dot :file "images/example-division.png"
   digraph{
   1; 2; 3; 4; A; C; F; D; E; B;

   {B, A} -> 1 [color = blue]
   C -> {1, 2} [color = blue]
   F -> 4 [color = blue]

{D} -> {1, 2, 3, 4} [color=blue]

Œ±, Œ≤, Œ≥, Œ¥ [style=filled];

Œ± -> {1, 4} [color=purple, style=dashed]
Œ± -> {A, B} [color=red, style=dotted]

Œ≥ -> {1, 2} [color=purple, style=dashed]
Œ≥ -> {C, E,B} [color=red, style=dotted]

Œ≤ -> {1, 2} [color=purple, style=dashed]
Œ≤ -> {A, C, B} [color=red, style=dotted]

Œ¥ -> {4} [color=purple, style=dashed]
Œ¥ -> {F, E} [color=red, style=dotted]
;
label="‚ÄúRed R‚Äù ‚®æ ‚ÄúBlue R‚ï≤S‚Äù  ‚äÜ  ‚ÄúPurple S‚Äù
       Walking red then blue, can always be done in one go as purple (‚Ä¢ÃÄ·¥ó‚Ä¢ÃÅ)Ÿà
       Moreover, this is the most possible (letters-to-numbers) blue with this property!"
}
#+end_src

   #+RESULTS:
   [[file:images/example-division.png]]

   Notice that we do *not* have /E ‚ü∂ 4/ since there are some ways to get to /E/ but
   not to get to /4/.

   ( Moreover, notice that we have /D ‚ü∂ 3/ even though /D/ is not reachable via any
   red or purple; which is fine since we want the most blue with the stated
   property. However, if our relations are finite, with infinite point sets,
   then their residuals would become infinite relations; to avoid this (e.g.,
   for computer implementations), one can insist on drawing blue only from
   reachable elements thereby obtaining $\mathsf{ran}\, R ‚®æ (R ‚ï≤ S)$, for a
   relational definition of ‚Äòrange‚Äô ---the resulting operation is known as a
   /restricted residual/. See [[https://core.ac.uk/download/pdf/82223799.pdf][here]] for more. )

   Notice that division /internalises/ inclusion: $R ‚äÜ S \quad‚â°\quad \Id ‚äÜ  R ‚ï≤
   S$.

      #+begin_src dot :file "images/example-self-division.png"
   digraph{
   1; 2; 3; 4;
Œ±, Œ≤, Œ≥, Œ¥ [style=filled];

// 3 [pos="9,9!"];

// 45 [style=invisible]
// 45 -> 4, 1, 2, 3 [color = cyan, style=invisible]
// Œ¥ -> Œ± [style = invisible]

Œ± -> {1, 4} [color=purple, style=dashed]
Œ≤ -> {1, 2} [color=purple, style=dashed]
Œ≥ -> {1, 2} [color=purple, style=dashed]
Œ¥ -> {4} [color=purple, style=dashed]

1 -> 1 [color = cyan]
2 -> 2, 1 [color = cyan]
3 -> 1,2,3,4 [color = cyan]
4 -> 4 [color = cyan]


;
label="‚ÄúPurple S‚Äù ‚®æ ‚ÄúCyan S‚ï≤S‚Äù  ‚äÜ  ‚ÄúPurple S‚Äù
               Walking purple then cyan, can always be done in one go as purple (‚Ä¢ÃÄ·¥ó‚Ä¢ÃÅ)Ÿà
               Moreover, this is the most possible cyan (among numbers) with this property!
               ( Self-division cyan is always a preorder. )"
}
#+end_src

#+RESULTS:
[[file:images/example-self-division.png]]

As mentioned in the example, it can be proven that $S ‚ï≤ S$ is always a preorder;
moreover, $P$ is preorder precisely when $P ‚ï≤ P = P$ and this is known as
‚Äúinclusion from below‚Äù.
- $‚â§ ‚ï≤ ‚â§ \;=\; ‚â§$
- $Œû ‚ï≤ Œû \;=\; Œû$ for any equivalence Œû ---including equality ‚Äò=‚Äô.
  # - ? $S Àò \;=\; S ‚ï± \sim \Id \;=\; \sim\Id ‚ï≤ S$ ---converse is a residual
- $‚àà ‚ï≤ ‚àà \;=\; ‚äÜ$
- $‚àà ‚ï≤ S : ‚Ñô X ‚Üî Y$ relates a set $A : ‚Ñô X$ to all of its $S : X ‚Üî Y$
  /shared/ successors; it is related to what is called a ‚Äúpolar‚Äù.
  + E.g., $‚àà ‚ï≤ ‚â§$ relates sets to their upper bounds.

For $f, g$ functions, $a (f ‚ï≤ g) c \;‚â°\; ‚àÄ x ‚Ä¢ f(x) = a ‚áí g(x) = c$; i.e., the
inputs /f/-yielding $a$ are /g/-yield $c$. That is, *[[green: things with f-feature ‚Äòa‚Äô
also have g-feature ‚Äòc‚Äô ]]* ---if we think of functions as projecting, exposing,
features (which they do!).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Suppose $R$ is a relation between objects and their properties.
Then two particularly common questions arise whenever
has a subset $A$ of objects ---or a subset $B$ of properties:
| /Which of the properties do all of the objects of $A$ have in common?/ |
| and, /Which objects enjoy all the properties of $B$?/                  |

Each is formulated as /[[red: A„Äî‚àà‚ï≤R„Äï?]]/ and /[[red: B„Äî‚àà‚ï≤RÀò„Äï?]]/ with unknowns [[red:?]].

The operations $Œõ(‚àà‚ï≤R)$ and $Œõ(‚àà‚ï≤RÀò)$ are then known as
/the upper (resp. lower) bound cone functionals/
or /polars/.
+ For a /relation/ $R : X ‚ÜîY$, its *power transpose* is the /function/
  $ŒõR : X ‚Üí ‚Ñô Y$ that sends each source value to the /set/ of its
  associated target values: $ŒõR(x) = \{y ‚ùô x „ÄîR„Äï y\}$.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

In case it's not clear, *[[orange: since S‚ï≤S is a preorder for any S, we can
(pre)order things according to _any_ relation we want]]* *[[brown:morever, S‚ï≤S ‚à© (S‚ï≤S)Àò
is then the largest equivalence contained in that (pre)order]].*

In-fact, since it is such a commonly occurring event, it has its own
name, *the symmetric quotient of /R/ and /S/* is
\[ R ‚ï≥ S \quad=\quad R‚ï≤S ‚à© (R ‚ï≤ S)Àò \]
Symmetric quotients give us a form of extensionality:
\[x „ÄîR ‚ï≥ S„Äï y \quad‚â°\quad (‚àÄ z ‚Ä¢ z „ÄîR„Äï x  \;‚â°\; z „ÄîS„Äï y)\]

Examples:
+  Relations, in contrast to functions, are mainly introduced in order to assign
  not precisely one result but a ---possibly empty--- /set/ of results, so that
  they are often conceived as set-valued functions.

  * $ŒõS \;=\; S Àò ‚ï≥ ‚àà$ relates an element to its set of /S/-successors; this
    is known as ‚Äúthe power transpose‚Äù mapping. Note that $Œõ : (X ‚Üî Y) ‚Üí X ‚Üí ‚Ñô Y$.

    This is just set comprehension concerning the set assigned to an argument:
    $ŒõS(x) \;=\; \{ y \,‚ùô\, x „ÄîS„Äï y\}$.

    Œõ is uniquely characterised by: /For any mapping f, and any relation R,/
    $\quad f = ŒõR
    \quad‚â°\quad f‚®æ‚ààÀò = R$.

    Due to typing reasons, the power transpose cannot preserve
    composition. However, we do have $Œõ(R) ‚®æ \mathsf{E}(S) \;=\; Œõ(R ‚®æ S)$ where /E/
    is the existential image mapping.

  * $\mathsf{E}\, S \;=\; (SÀò‚®æ‚àà) √ó ‚àà$ relates a set of elements to their set of
    images; it is known as ‚Äúthe existential image‚Äù mapping.  Note that
    $\mathsf{E} : (X ‚Üî Y) ‚Üí ‚Ñô X ‚Üí ‚Ñô Y$.

    As the name suggests, for every constituent of the result, there /exists/ an
    argument for which it is the /image/.  Or, using unions: $\mathsf{E}S(A) \;=\;
    (‚à™ x \,‚ùô\, x ‚àà A ‚Ä¢ ŒõS(x)) \;=\; \{ y \,‚ùô\, (‚àÉ x \,‚ùô\, x ‚àà A ‚Ä¢ x„ÄîS„Äï y) \}$.
    ( In Z notation, this is known as the /relational image/ and denoted
    /S‚¶áA‚¶à = E S A/. )

    $\mathsf{E}$ preserves composition and identity, and it ‚Äòrepresents‚Äô its
    argument: $‚ààÀò ‚®æ R \;=\; \mathsf{E}\,R ‚®æ ‚àà Àò$.

+ $‚àà ‚ï≥ ‚àº‚àà$ relates a set to its complement
+ $\Id ‚ï≥ ‚àà$ wraps a value as a singleton set
+ $‚àà ‚ï≥ ‚àà = \Id$ ---this is set extensionality

  The relation /‚àà ‚ï≥ S/ is always surjective,
  and /S = ‚àà ‚®æ (‚àà ‚ï≥ S)/ whereas /‚àºS = ‚àâ ‚®æ (‚àà ‚ï≥ S)/.


#+begin_details ‚Äúsymmetric‚Äù?
The symmetric quotient is /not/ a symmetric relation, nor need it even be
homogeneous.  The name describes that it is defined in some symmetric way.

It takes two possibly heterogenous relations with coinciding source
and yields a relation from the first's target to the second's target; i.e.,
it's type is /(X ‚Üî Y) ‚Üí (X ‚Üî Z) ‚Üí (Y ‚Üî Z)/.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Begin defined in terms of ‚Äú‚â°‚Äù, symmetric quotients serve to internalise
equality at the relational level and can be used to simulate set comprehensions
and comparisons.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Theorems
1. /S ‚ï≥ R = (R ‚ï≥ S)Àò/ ---equivalence is symmetric, but to flip the order of
   arguments bound by the ‚àÄ, we need the converse
2. /R ‚ï≥ S = R ‚ï≤ S ‚à© ‚àºR ‚ï≤ ‚àºS/ ---the equivalence via R and S is the conjunction
   of everything in R being in S /and/ everything outside of R being outside of S.
3. /‚àºR ‚ï≥ ‚àºS = R ‚ï≥ S/  ---this is essentially double negation ;-)
4. /Id ‚äÜ R ‚ï≥ R/ ---equivalence is a reflexive relation ;-)

   In-fact, /R ‚ï≥ R/ is always an equivalence relation;
   whereas /R ‚ï≥ S/ is difunctional.

   Furthermore, when /R/ is surjective we have /R ‚ï≥ R ‚äÜ RÀò ‚®æ R/ --and equality when
   /R/ is additionally injective. More generally, /R Àò ‚®æ S ‚äÜ R ‚ï≥ S/ when /both/ /R/ and
   /S/ are injective.

5. /R ‚®æ (R ‚ï≥ R) = R/ ---read /‚Äúx is sent to some value R-indistinguishable from y‚Äù ‚â°
   ‚Äúx is sent to y‚Äù/

   More generally: /R ‚®æ (R ‚ï≥ S) = S ‚à© ‚ä§‚®æ(R ‚ï≥ S) ‚äÜ S/ ---whence, we have equality
   when /R ‚ï≥ S/ is surjective.

   However, we do have: /(R ‚ï≥ R) ‚®æ (R ‚ï≥ S) = R ‚ï≥ S/.

6. /(Q ‚ï≥ R) ‚®æ (R ‚ï≥ S) = (Q ‚ï≥ S) ‚à© (Q ‚ï≥ R)‚®æ‚ä§ = (Q ‚ï≥ S) ‚à© ‚ä§‚®æ(R ‚ï≥ S) ‚äÜ Q ‚ï≥ S/
   ---whence, we have equality when /Q ‚ï≥ R/ is total /or/ /R ‚ï≥ S/ is surjective.

   Consequently: /R ‚ï≥ S ‚äÜ (Q √ó R) ‚ï≤ (Q ‚ï≥ S)/

   Incidentally: /R ‚ï≥ S ‚äÜ (Q ‚ï≥ R) ‚ï≥ (Q ‚ï≥ S)/
   ---with equality when /Q ‚ï≥ R and Q ‚ï≥ S/ are surjective.

7. Cancellation: /R ‚ï≥ S ‚äÜ (Q ‚®æ R) ‚ï≥ (Q ‚®æ S)/ ---with equality when /Q/ is a
   surjective mapping.

8. Distributivity: For every mapping /f/,
   + /f ‚®æ (R ‚ï≥ S) = (R ‚®æ fÀò) ‚ï≥ S/
   + /(R ‚ï≥ S) ‚®æ fÀò = R ‚ï≥ (S ‚®æ fÀò)/ ---this is the transpose of the previous one
     ;-)

9. The relation /(R ‚®æ ‚àà) ‚ï≥ (R ‚®æ S)/ is always surjective.

#+end_details

Then, $P$ is an order precisely when
$P ‚ï≤ P = P$ and
$\Id \;=\; P ‚ï≥ P$ ---this is known as ‚Äúindirect equality‚Äù.
+ More generally, $\Id \;‚äÜ\; S ‚ï≥ S$ and,
  /S/ is antisymmetric ‚â° $S ‚ï≥ S \;‚äÜ\; \Id$.
   # + Incidentally, if /S/ is a univalent surjection then $S ‚ï≥ S \;‚äÜ\; \Id$.
   # + From the previous two, we see that the /homogenous/ property of antisymmetry
   #   coincides with the /heterogenous/ property of being a univalent surjection!
   #   This looks wrong?
   #
  Moreover, Œû is an equivalence exactly when Œû = Œû ‚ï≥ Œû.

+ /R injective  ‚â°  R ‚äÜ R ‚ï≥ Id/; i.e., if /R/ associates /x/ with /y/, then
  the /R/ preimage of /y/ are exactly /x/.
  + /R injective ‚áî R ‚®æ R Àò ‚äÜ Id ‚áî R Àò ‚äÜ R ‚ï≤ Id/; i.e., /‚Äúif y is the image of x,
    then every preimage of y must coincide with x‚Äù/.
  # + /R univalent  ‚â° R ‚äÜ R Àò ‚ï≥ Id/ ? This looks wrong.
  # + /R univalent ‚áî R Àò ‚®æ R ‚äÜ Id ‚áî R ‚äÜ R Àò ‚ï≤ Id/; i.e., /‚Äúif x has image y, then
  #  every image of x must coincide with y‚Äù./

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Finally, with residuals we can lift De Morgan's rule to the pure relational
language ---since the negation flips the ‚àÉ of ‚®æ with the ‚àÄ of ‚ï≤---
\[ \sim(R ‚®æ S) \quad=\quad R Àò ‚ï≤ \sim S \]
\[ \sim(R ‚ï≤ S) \quad=\quad R Àò ‚®æ \sim S \]

For example, we can use these rules to rephrase what it means to be total.

|   | /R/ is total                                                  |
| /=/ | /R ‚®æ ‚ä§ = ‚ä§/                                                   |
| /=/ | /‚àº(R ‚®æ ‚ä§) = ‚àº ‚ä§/                                              |
| /=/ | /R Àò ‚ï≤ ‚ä• = ‚ä•/                                                 |
| /=/ | /‚ÄúFor every point, it is not the case that it has no image.‚Äù/ |

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Residuals are *green:the* tool to translate universal quantifications into
(complement-free) relation-algebraic formulae.

* Graphs                                                               :Lisp:
  :PROPERTIES:
  :CUSTOM_ID: Graphs
  :END:

The first two sections below follow
/‚ÄúData Structures and Algorithms‚Äù by Aho, Hopcroft, and Ullman./

Let's make a neato graph rendering mechanism so we can actually orange:see our graphs.
#+begin_src emacs-lisp :title "Graph API"
;; A Graph is implemented as a list of adjaceny lists; i.e., a list of lists;
;; i.e., an alist.  However, to actually be practical, we allow the adjaencey
;; lists to have meta-data.
;;
;; Each adjaencey list can be in either metadata-style or not, the latter means
;; it is a node and a list of its neighbours, whereas the former means it is a
;; node followed by an alternating ‚Äú:key value‚Äù elements only, with ‚Äú:to‚Äù
;; followed by the neighbours of the node. Other keys include ‚Äú:node-color,
;; :children-color‚Äù, and most importantly, any other meta-data the user wants
;; nodes to carry: For example, ‚Äú:visited?‚Äù for searching or ‚Äú:weight‚Äù for
;; weighted graphs.  Since we're not pacaking up our graphs into structs, we
;; obtain this flexibility in terms of the kinds of meta-data allowed.

;; Helpers for readability
(defun set-vertex-data (G x key value) (plist-put (cdr (assoc x G)) key value))
(defun get-vertex-data (G x key) (plist-get (cdr (assoc x G)) key)) ;; Yields ‚Äònil‚Äô when ‚Äòkey‚Äô is not present.
(defun successors (G x)
  (let* ((ddata (cdr (assoc x G)))
         (to-s (plist-get ddata :to)))
    (pcase to-s ('(nil) nil)
                ('nil ddata)
                (nbrs nbrs))))
(defun predecessors (G x)
  (mapcar #'car (--filter (member x (successors G (car it))) G)))

;; Below I roll-out my own mini-graph-API, but there are others...
;; ‚Üí ‚Äúconcept mapping‚Äù with Org-brain, uses Org as interface, not lisp:
;;   ‚üÖ This is likely where I'd run into graphs and want to use them explicitly! ‚üÜ
;;    https://github.com/Kungsgeten/org-brain
;;    https://github.com/Kungsgeten/org-brain-export
;;    https://github.com/swhalemwo/obvz
;; ‚Üí Common Lisp library: https://quickref.common-lisp.net/cl-digraph.html
;; ‚Üí Emacs Lisp library: https://github.com/storax/graph.el
#+end_src

#+begin_src emacs-lisp
(cl-defun show-graph (G &key
                        (type 'png)
                        (file (format "%s.%s" (make-temp-name "~/Downloads/graph_") type))
                        (text "")
                        (cmd "dot"))
  "Given a graph G, an alist of nodes-neighbours, draw it.

G may be provided as a super simple alist; or with data for each node
in which case G is an alist of plists, with the neighbours :to;
in-turn the neighbours can be a simple list, or have meta-data
such as the weight or color of the edge  to edge neighbour.

TYPE: Either PNG or SVG
   Use HTML-renderable SVG to avoid having to copy-paste/save-refer PNGs
FILE: File name, must end  with extension being TYPE.
   A random name ‚Äú~/Dowbnloads/graph_‚ãØ‚Äù is used as default.
TEXT: Any header text to appear in the graph.
CMD: The command, eg Ã®‚Äúdot‚Äù or ‚Äúneato‚Äù, to produce the graph
   Neato produces graphs with less whitespace than does Dot,
   but otherwise the same thing.

If you decide to use SVGs instead of PNGs, then the Org src should have as header
‚Äú:wrap export html ‚Äù; otherwise use ‚Äú‚Äù.

A cookbook of Dot examples:
https://renenyffenegger.ch/notes/tools/Graphviz/examples/index
https://graphs.grevian.org/example
"
  (org-babel-execute:dot
   (format "digraph {label = \"%s\"\nlabelloc = \"t\"\nrankdir=TB\n%s}"
           text
           (loop for (node . data) in G
                 for node-color = (or (plist-get data :node-color) "black")
                 for edge-color = (or (plist-get data :edge-color) "black")
                 ;; The extra " are so that we can escape unicode names for Dot
                 concat
                 (concat (format "\n\"%s\"[color=\"%s\"];" node node-color)
                         (loop for c in (successors G node)
                               for c‚Ä≤ = (if (listp c) c (list c))
                               for color = (or (plist-get (cdr c‚Ä≤) :color) edge-color)
                               concat (format "\n\"%s\" -> \"%s\" [color=\"%s\"]"
                                              node (car c‚Ä≤) color)))))
  `((:file . ,file)
     ;; Use HTML-renderable SVG to avoid having to copy-paste/save-refer PNGs
     ,@(when (equal 'svg type) '((:cmdline . "-Tsvg")))
    ;; Neato produces graphs with less whitespace than does Dot, but otherwise the same thing.
     (:cmd . ,cmd)))
  ;; Return file name or dump entire contents here
  (if (equal 'png type)
      (format "[[file:%s]]" file)
    (org-file-contents file)))
#+end_src

:Another-example:
# +begin_src emacs-lisp  :results replace :wrap export html :exports code
#+begin_src emacs-lisp
;; Super simple graph representation
;; (show-graph '((* + /) (+ a b) (/ + c)))

;; With coloured edges
;; (show-graph '((* :to (+ (/ :color blue)) :edge-color pink) (+ a b) (/ + c)))

;; Using other bells and whistles
(show-graph '( (* :to (+ /) :node-color "darkblue" :edge-color green)
               ;; Remaining have no meta-data, for brevity
               (+ a b)
               (/ + c))
              :type 'svg
              ; :type 'png
              ; :file "images/example-graph.png"
              :text "‚üÖ (a + b) * ( (a + b) / c ) ‚üÜ \n ‚üÖ DAGs Allow Sharing ‚üÜ")
#+end_src
:End:

#+begin_center
[[badge:Elisp|CheatSheet|success|https://alhassy.github.io/ElispCheatSheet/CheatSheet.pdf|Gnu-Emacs][Elisp cheat sheet]]
#+end_center

** COMMENT Transitive closure
   :PROPERTIES:
   :CUSTOM_ID: Transitive-closure
   :END:

We can effectively calculate the transitive closure $R^+$ by /preserving
transitivity locally/: In each step, a node $x$ is chosen, and its predecessors
$RÀò‚¶á\{x\}‚¶à$ are joined to its successors $R‚¶á\{x\}‚¶à and so partial transitivity
is established in the sense that every pair of gray:consecutive arrows joining each
other at $x$ gives rise to a blue:single new arrow.
   #+begin_parallel 2
#+begin_src emacs-lisp  :wrap export html :exports results
(show-graph '((l‚ÇÅ x) (l‚ÇÇ x) (x r‚ÇÅ r‚ÇÇ r‚ÇÉ)) :type 'svg)
#+end_src
